إلا أن السنة عندنا قد تقع على سُنَّة النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام وغيره. وقال الشافعي رحمه الله : مطلقها : طريقة النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم. قال ذلك في أرش ما دون النفس في النساء: إنه لا يتنصف إلى الثلث؛ لقول سعيد بن المسيب رضي الله عنه : السنة. وقال ذلك في قتل الحر بالعبد " وعندنا : هي مطلقة، لا قيد فيها، فلا تُقيَّد بلا دليل. وكان السلف يقولون : سُنَّة العُمَرَيْن والسنن نوعان: سنةُ الهدى وتاركها يستوجب إساءة وكراهية. والزوائد، وتاركها لا يستوجب إساءةً ، كسير النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام

(1) قوله : لقول سعيد بن المسيب السنة ابن أبي شيبة عن ربيعة بن أبي عبد الرحمن قال: قلت لسعيد بن المسيب : كم في هذه من المرأة؟ يعني الخنصر، فقال: عشر من الإبل ، قال :قلت وفي هذه ؟ يعني الخنصر والتي تليها، قال: عشرون قلت : فهؤلاء؟ يعني الثلاثة، قال: ثلاثون قال: قلت: ففي هؤلاء، وأومأ إلى الأربع؟ قال عشرون قال قلت حين ألمت ،جراحتها وعظمت مصيبتها: كان الأقل لأرشها ؟ قال : أعراقي أنت؟ قال: قلت عالم متثبت أو جاهل متعلم؟ فقال: يابن أخي! السنة».

(2) قوله : وقال ذلك في قتل الحر بالعبد... [بياض في الأصل].

(3) قوله : كان السلف يقولون : سُنَّة العُمَرَين..... [ بياض في الأصل].

(4) وفي نسخ: كسنن. سائد.
Except that Sunnah according to us may apply to the sunnah of the Prophet (peace be upon him) and others. Al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) said: Its absolute meaning is the way of the Prophet. He said that regarding the blood money for wounds less than life for women: that it does not halve until one-third, due to the statement of Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib: 'The Sunnah.' And he said that regarding killing a free man for a slave. According to us, it is absolute, unrestricted, so it is not restricted without evidence. The Salaf used to say: 'The Sunnah of the two 'Umars.' And the Sunnahs are of two types: Sunnat al-Huda, and its abandoner incurs blame and dislike. And the Zawa'id, and its abandoner does not incur blame, like the walking of the Prophet.

(1) His saying: 'Due to the statement of Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib: The Sunnah' – Ibn Abi Shayba narrated from Rabi'a ibn Abi 'Abd al-Rahman: I said to Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib: How much is in this? – meaning the little finger – he said: Ten camels. I said: And in this? – meaning the little finger and the next – he said: Twenty. I said: These – meaning the three – he said: Thirty. I said: And in these, gesturing to the four – he said: Twenty. I said: Her wound being severe and her calamity great, was the least for her blood money? He said: Are you an Iraqi? I said: A knowledgeable person seeking confirmation or an ignorant person seeking knowledge? He said: O son of my brother! The Sunnah.

(2) His saying: 'And he said that regarding killing a free man for a slave... [blank in original]'

(3) His saying: 'The Salaf used to say: The Sunnah of the two 'Umars... [blank in original]'

(4) And in some copies: 'like the sunan.' [variant]

Commentary
This passage is a discussion on the definition of the term 'Sunnah' in Hanafi usul. The author clarifies that in the Hanafi school, 'Sunnah' is not restricted exclusively to the actions of the Prophet (peace be upon him) but can also refer to the practices of other righteous figures, such as the Companions. This is in contrast to Imam al-Shafi'i, who holds that the absolute term 'Sunnah' refers specifically to the Prophet's way. The Hanafi position is that the term is absolute (mutlaq) and should not be restricted without evidence. The author supports this by mentioning that the early Muslims (Salaf) used phrases like 'Sunnat al-'Umarayn' (the Sunnah of the two 'Umars, i.e., Abu Bakr and 'Umar). The passage then divides Sunnah into two types: Sunnat al-Huda, which is recommended and abandoning it is blameworthy, and Sunnat al-Zawa'id, which consists of the Prophet's habitual actions (like his manner of walking) that are not legally significant. This distinction is important in Hanafi fiqh for determining the level of obligation and blame. The footnote provides a narration from Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib about blood money for fingers, illustrating the use of the term 'Sunnah' by a Companion.

Key Terms
سنة (Sunnah): In Hanafi usul, the term 'Sunnah' can refer to the practice of the Prophet (PBUH) or other early Muslims, as used by the Salaf.
سنة الهدى (Sunnat al-Huda): The recommended practices that are part of the religious guidance; abandoning them is disliked and blameworthy.
سنة الزوائد (Sunnat al-Zawa'id): The Prophet's habitual actions that are not specifically religious; abandoning them incurs no blame.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, why is the term 'Sunnah' not restricted to the actions of the Prophet alone?
A) Because the Quran does not specify the term.
B) Because the Salaf used it for the practices of others, e.g., 'Sunnat al-'Umarayn'.
C) Because al-Shafi'i restricted it without evidence.
D) Because it would lead to contradiction with other sources.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, 'Sunnah' is an absolute term covering the practices of the Prophet and the Salaf, divided into Sunnat al-Huda (blameworthy to abandon) and Sunnat al-Zawa'id (no blame).

Hanafi Definition of Sunnah and Its Types
Defining Sunnah and its types in Hanafi Usul

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of Sunnah, classify Sunnah into Sunnat al-Huda and Sunnat al-Zawa'id, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Sunnah in Hanafi usul includes the Prophet's and Companions' practices, divided into two types with different legal consequences.

In this passage from Bazdawi's Usul, the author clarifies the Hanafi position on what constitutes Sunnah. Unlike the Shafi'i view that restricts Sunnah to the Prophet's way, Hanafis expansively include the practices of the Rightly Guided Caliphs and other Companions. This definition has direct impact on rulings such as blood money (arsh) and the adhan. Let's unpack the classification and its evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined: Sunnah
The passage begins by stating the Hanafi position: 'Sunnah may apply to the Prophet and others.' Note that this is a usul definition, not a fiqh one.
The Salaf used 'Sunnah al-ʿUmarayn' – the Sunnah of the two ʿUmars.
2
Recognize the Shafi'i objection
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that absolute 'Sunnah' refers only to the Prophet's method. The passage cites his argument from the arsh case and the case of killing a free man for a slave.
Shafi'i's proof includes Sa'id ibn al-Musayyab's statement 'Sunnah'.
3
Understand the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response: 'It is absolute, with no restriction, so it is not restricted without evidence.' The term is unrestricted, and the burden of proof lies on those who restrict it.
This is a classic usul argument about the default being generality.
4
Distinguish the two types of Sunnah
Sunnat al-Huda: abandoning earns blame and dislike. Sunnat al-Zawa'id: abandoning does not earn blame (e.g., the Prophet's personal habits).
Example: adhan rulings vary because some parts are Sunnat al-Huda and some are Zawa'id.
5
Apply to examples from adhan
The passage notes that in the adhan chapter, scholars differed: sometimes it is disliked, sometimes wrong, sometimes no harm. This variation stems from the two types of Sunnah.
The ruling of 'he should repeat' indicates obligation (wujub), but that is not the default for Sunnah; it applies only when a Sunnah is elevated to wajib by context.

قال ذلك في أرش ما دون النفس في النساء: إنه لا يتنصف إلى الثلث؛ لقول سعيد بن المسيب رضي الله عنه : السنة).

وكان السلف يقولون : سُنَّة العُمَرَيْن
والسنن نوعان:
- سنةُ الهدى وتاركها يستوجب إساءة وكراهية.
والزوائد، وتاركها لا يستوجب إساءةً ، كسير النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام في لباسه وقيامه وقعوده
وعلى هذا: مسائل باب الأذان من كتاب الصلاة ،اختلفت فقيل مرةً: يكره، ومرة أساء، ومرة: لا بأس به؛ لما قلنا.
وإذا قيل : يُعيدُ : فذلك من حكم الوجوب.
وأما النفل : فما يُثاب المرء على فعله، ولا يعاقب على تركه. ولذلك قلنا: إن ما زاد على القصر من صلاة السفر: نفل، والنفل شرع دائماً ، فلذلك جعلناه من العزائم، ولذلك صح قاعداً وراكباً؛ لأنه على ما شُرع يلازم العجز ، لا محالة، فلازَمَ اليُسر، وهذا القدر من جنس الرخص.
وقال الشافعي رحمه الله : لمَّا شُرعُ النفل على هذا الوصف: وجب أن يبقى كذلك غير لازم وقد غيّرتُم أنتم ، حيث قلتُم باللزوم، وقلتُ أنا : إن ما لم يفعل بعد فهو فيه، فبطل المؤدى حكماً له، كالمظنون.
وقلنا نحن إن ما أداه فقد صار لغيره مسلّما إليه، وحق غيره محترم، مضمون عليه إتلافه، ولا سبيل إليه إلا بإلزام الباقي، وهما أمران متعارضان، أعني : المؤدَّى، وغير المؤدَّى، فوجب الترجيح لما قلنا بالاحتياط في العبادات.
وهو كالنذر: صار الله تعالى تسميةً لا فعلاً، ثم وجب؛ لصيانته ابتداء الفعل، فلأن يجب لصيانة ابتداء الفعل : بقاؤه أولى.
والسنن كثيرةٌ في باب الصلاة والحج وغير ذلك.
But according to us, 'Sunnah' may apply to the Sunnah of the Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him, and others. Al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, said: Its absolute meaning is the way of the Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him. He said that regarding the blood-money for wounds less than the soul (life) in women: that it does not reach half of one-third, due to the statement of Sa'id ibn al-Musayyab, may Allah be pleased with him: 'Sunnah.' And he said that regarding killing a free man for a slave. According to us, it is absolute, with no restriction, so it is not restricted without evidence. And the Salaf used to say: 'Sunnah of the two 'Umars.' Sunnahs are of two types: Sunnat al-Huda (Sunnah of guidance) – its abandoner earns blame and disapproval; and al-Zawa'id (supererogatory practices) – its abandoner does not earn blame, like the manner of the Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him, in his dress, standing, and sitting. Based on this: issues of the chapter of the adhan from the Book of Prayer – they differed: sometimes it was said: it is disliked, sometimes: he did wrong, sometimes: there is no harm, due to what we said. And if it is said: 'he should repeat,' that is from the ruling of obligation. As for Nafl: it is that for which a person is rewarded for doing it and not punished for abandoning it. Therefore we said: Whatever is added beyond the shortened (prayer) in the prayer of travel is Nafl, and Nafl is perpetually legislated, so we made it from the 'Azaim (binding resolves), and therefore it is valid while sitting and riding; because as it is legislated, it is necessarily associated with inability, so it is associated with ease, and this amount is from the type of concessions. Al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, said: Since Nafl was legislated on this description, it must remain as such, non-binding, and you have changed it where you said it is binding. And I said: What has not been done after it is still in it, so the performed part is annulled in ruling, like the probable (speculative). And we said: What he has performed has become delivered to another, and the right of another is sacred, and its destruction is guaranteed, and there is no way to it except by obligating the remaining. They are two conflicting matters, namely the performed and the non-performed, so preference was given to what we said due to precaution in acts of worship. And it is like a vow: it became by the name of Allah, not by action, then it became obligatory for its preservation from the start of the act, so it is more fitting that for the preservation of the start of the act, its continuation is more appropriate. And the Sunan are many in the chapter of prayer, Hajj, and other than that.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage from Bazdawi's Usul defines two key terms in Hanafi fiqh: Sunnah and Nafl, with their subdivisions and legal implications. The author first addresses the definition of 'Sunnah' in response to Imam al-Shafi'i. According to the Hanafi school, 'Sunnah' is not confined solely to the actions and sayings of the Prophet (peace and blessings be upon him); it also encompasses the practices of the Rightly Guided Caliphs (Umar and Abu Bakr) and other Companions, as evidenced by the Salaf calling it 'Sunnah al-'Umarayn'. The Shafi'i position restricts absolute 'Sunnah' to the Prophet's way only. The Hanafi argument rests on the absolute nature of the word, which cannot be restricted without evidence. Two practical applications illustrate this: the calculation of blood money (arsh) for fingers in women (where Shafi'i relies on Sa'id ibn al-Musayyab's statement 'Sunnah' to limit the amount to one-third), and the case of a free man killed for a slave. The passage then classifies Sunnah into two types: Sunnat al-Huda (like the sunan of prayer), abandoning which is blameworthy and disliked, and Sunnat al-Zawa'id (the Prophet's personal habits), abandoning which is not blameworthy. Examples are given from the adhan (call to prayer) where the ruling varies between disliked, wrong, and permissible. The second part discusses Nafl (voluntary acts). The Hanafi position is that once a Nafl act is begun, it becomes binding (lazim) and must be completed, similar to a vow (nadh). This is justified by the principle of precaution in worship (ihtiyat) and the idea that the started act becomes a right of Allah that must be fulfilled. Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees, arguing that a Nafl act should remain non-binding as it was legislated, and if abandoned, the performed part becomes void. The Hanafi response is that the performed part has been delivered to Allah's right, and to preserve it, the remaining part must be completed, giving preference to precaution. The passage concludes by noting the abundance of Sunan in various chapters of fiqh.

Key Terms
سنة (Sunnah): According to Hanafis, a legally recommended act that includes the Prophet's practice and that of the Companions, divided into Sunnat al-Huda and Sunnat al-Zawa'id.
نفل (Nafl): A voluntary act of worship for which one is rewarded but not punished for omission, but which becomes binding once started in the Hanafi school.
سنة الهدى (Sunnat al-Huda): Sunnah of guidance whose abandonment earns blame and dislike (karahah).
سنة الزوائد (Sunnat al-Zawa'id): Sunnah of supererogatory practices, like the Prophet's personal habits, whose abandonment is not blameworthy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following is considered Sunnah?
A) Only the Prophet's actions
B) The Prophet's and the Companions' actions
C) Only the actions of the Caliphs
D) Any recommended action without prophetic precedent

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, Sunnah is not restricted to the Prophet's practice; it includes the practices of the Companions, and is classified into two types: Sunnat al-Huda and Sunnat al-Zawa'id.

The Nature of Nafl and Its Binding Effect
Defining Nafl and its binding nature in Hanafi Usul

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that a nafl act becomes binding (lazim) once started, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
According to Hanafis, starting a nafl binds you to complete it, just like a vow.

In the second part of this passage, Bazdawi tackles a key controversy between Hanafis and Shafi'is: does a voluntary act of worship become obligatory once begun? The Hanafi position, based on the principle of precaution and analogy with vows, insists that it does. The Shafi'i school maintains that the act remains voluntary. Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
The issue is whether a nafl (voluntary) act of worship becomes binding (lazim) once started.
2
State the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i argues: since nafl was legislated as non-binding, it should remain non-binding. If abandoned, the performed part becomes void.
The Shafi'i dalil: the original legislation of nafl implies perpetual non-binding nature.
3
State the Hanafi position
The Hanafi position: once started, nafl becomes binding and must be completed.
4
Analyze the Hanafi evidence
The Hanafi evidence includes: (1) the performed part becomes a right of Allah that must be protected; (2) analogy with a vow (nadh); (3) the principle of precaution (ihtiyat) in acts of worship.
The passage says: 'What he has performed has become delivered to another, and the right of another is sacred.'
5
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: if one starts a nafl prayer, Hanafis require completion; Shafi'is do not. Also, the permissibility of praying nafl while sitting or riding without excuse is affected.
Hanafis consider nafl to be from the 'Azaim (binding resolves) and allow it while sitting/riding, which aligns with its ease.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling

The Perfect Real Concession (Rukhsah Haqiqah)
Classifies rukhsah into four types and details the first, most complete type

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the first type of real concession (rukhsah haqiqah) according to Hanafi usul and explain why the prohibition's ruling remains despite the permission.

Core Idea
The perfect concession permits an act while the prohibition's ruling remains intact; the sin is not lifted, only the act is allowed due to necessity.

In Hanafi legal theory, a concession (rukhsah) is not simply a removal of the prohibition; rather, it is a nuanced permission that coexists with the original ruling. This lesson unpacks the first and most complete type of real concession, where the prohibition and its penal effect (sin) both remain, yet the act is permitted due to an overriding right of the individual. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying concessions in cases of coercion and necessity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four-type classification
Recognize that rukhsah is divided into two real and two metaphorical types, each with degrees. The first real type is the most deserving of the name.
Memorize: two real (haqiqah) and two metaphorical (majaz), each with a superior subtype.
2
Define the first real type
The first real concession is 'permitted despite the existence of the prohibition and its ruling both remaining.' This means the act is allowed without removing the sin (ithm) associated with it.
Key phrase: 'قيام المُحرِّم، وقيام حكمه جميعاً' – both the prohibition and its ruling stand.
3
Analyze the coercion example
The classic case: one coerced to utter kufr may speak to save his life (concession), whereas the determination ('azimah) is to accept martyrdom. The prohibition of kufr remains, and its sin is not lifted; but the right of the slave over his own life outweighs temporarily.
Allah's right (faith) is not lost in meaning (tasdiq remains), and form is not entirely lost because faith was already performed.
4
Apply to the 'commanding good' example
Similarly, one who fears death for enjoining good may abandon it (concession) or persevere to martyrdom (determination). The ruling that forbids abandoning good remains, but the right to life permits the concession.
Bearing death with aim to disperse sinners makes one a mujahid; otherwise (hopeless duel) it is blameworthy.
5
Contrast with the exception
A warrior who duels knowing he will die without benefit is not following the concession but wasting his life. This shows the concession is only valid when there is a realistic chance of preserving a legitimate right.
The mujahid's case: his death serves to scatter the enemy; the hopeless duel does not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الرخص فأربعة أنواع: نوعان من الحقيقة أحدهما أحق من الآخر، ونوعان من المجاز، أحدهما أتم من الآخر. 1 - أما أحقُّ نوعي الحقيقة فما استُبيح مع قيام المُحرِّم، وقيام حكمه جميعاً، فهو الكامل في الرخصة. مثل المكره على إجراء كلمة الكفر أنه يُرخص له إجراؤها، : والعزيمة في الصبر حتى يُقتل ؛ لأن حرمة الكفر قائمة ؛ لوجوب حق الله تعالى في الإيمان، لكنه رُخص لعذر ، وهو أن حق العبد في نفسه يفوتُ بالقتل صورةً ،ومعنى، وحق الله تعالى لا يفوتُ معنى؛ لأن التصديق باق، ولا يفوتُ  ُصورةً من كل وجه؛ لأن أداء الإيمان قد صح. وليس التكرار بركن لكن في إجراء كلمة الكفر هتك لحقه ظاهراً، فكان له تقديم حق نفسه ؛ كرامةً له من الله تعالى، وإن شاء بَذَلَ نفسه في دينه ؛ لإقامة حقه ظاهراً، فهذا مشروع قُربةً، فبقي عزيمة، وصار بها مجاهداً. وكذلك الذي يأمر بالمعروف إذا خاف القتل: رُخص له في الترك ؛ لما قلنا من مراعاة حقه، وإن شاء صبر حتى يقتل، وهو العزيمة؛ لأن حق الله تعالى في حرمة المنكر باق، وفي بذل نفسه : إقامة المعروف؛ لأن الظاهر أنه إذا قُتل : تَفرَّق جَمْعُ الفَسَقَة، وما كان غرضه إلا تفريق جمعهم، فبَذَلَ نفسه لذلك، فصار مجاهداً. بخلاف الغازي إذا بارز وهو يعلم أنه يُقتل من غير أن يُنكِيَ فيهم ؛ لأن جَمْعَهم لا يتفرق بسببه، فيصير مضيّعاً لدمه لا محتسباً مجاهداً.
As for the concessions (rukhas), they are four types: two types are real (haqiqah) – one is more deserving than the other – and two types are metaphorical (majaz) – one is more complete than the other. 1. The most deserving of the two real types is that which is permitted despite the existence of the prohibition and its ruling both remaining in full. It is the perfect concession. An example is the one compelled (mukrah) to utter a word of disbelief; he is granted a concession to utter it, whereas the determination ('azimah) is to be patient until killed – because the prohibition of disbelief remains due to the obligation of Allah's right in faith, but a concession is granted due to an excuse: the slave's right in his own life is lost in form and meaning by being killed, while Allah's right is not lost in meaning because attestation (tasdiq) remains, nor is it lost in form entirely because the performance of faith has already been valid. And repetition is not a pillar, but uttering a word of disbelief involves apparent violation of His right. So he is allowed to give precedence to his own right as a grace from Allah. If he wishes, he may sacrifice himself for his religion to establish Allah's right outwardly – this is legislated as an act of devotion (qurba), so it remains a determination ('azimah), and he becomes a mujahid thereby. Similarly, the one who enjoins good when he fears being killed: a concession is granted to him to abandon it for the reason we mentioned of considering his right. If he wishes, he may be patient until killed, and that is the determination because Allah's right in prohibiting evil remains. In sacrificing himself, he establishes good because apparently if he is killed, the group of sinners will disperse, and his only aim is to disperse them, so he sacrifices himself for that and becomes a mujahid. Unlike the warrior who duels when he knows he will be killed without inflicting damage on them, because his group will not disperse due to him, so he becomes a waster of his blood, not a reckoner or a mujahid.
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage defines the four types of rukhsah (concession) and elaborates on the first type, which is a real concession and the most perfect. The author states that rukhsah is divided into real (haqiqah) and metaphorical (majaz), each with two subtypes. The first real type is where the act is permitted even though the prohibition (tahrim) and its ruling (e.g., sin) both remain in effect. The example is the one coerced to utter kufr: the concession allows him to speak to save his life, while the 'azimah (determination) is to bear martyrdom. The analysis explains that Allah's right (faith) is not lost in essence (belief remains), but his own right (life) is lost completely if killed, so he may prefer his own right. However, if he chooses to be killed, that is also praiseworthy and he becomes a mujahid. Similarly, the person commanding good who fears death may abandon it (concession) or persevere (determination). The passage contrasts this with a soldier who fights in a hopeless manner without benefit – that is not a concession but a waste. The key point: the perfect rukhsah involves the concurrent existence of both the prohibition and its ruling, yet permission is given due to a valid excuse.

Key Terms
الرخصة (al-rukhsah): A concession that permits an act despite the prohibition's ruling remaining in place.
العزيمة (al-'azimah): The original ruling without concession; here, to persevere even to death.
المكره (al-mukrah): One who is coerced or compelled under threat; the coercion negates free choice but not the prohibition's ruling.
حقيقة (haqiqah): Literal or real meaning; here refers to a real concession where the original ruling is not metaphorically lifted.
مجاز (majaz): Metaphorical meaning; here refers to a concession that is not literally a permission in the full sense.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes the first type of real concession from other types?
A) The act becomes obligatory due to necessity.
B) The prohibition is completely lifted and the act becomes neutral.
C) The act is permitted while the prohibition and its ruling both remain.
D) The concession applies only to acts of worship, not daily life.

Key Takeaway
The perfect real concession (rukhsah haqiqah) permits an act without removing the original sin, balancing the right of the individual against the right of Allah.

Rukhsah Isqat in Qasr al-Salah and Fasting
Determining whether qasr salah is rukhsah isqat or not

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi criterion for distinguishing rukhsah isqat from rukhsah takhyir by analyzing the linguistic evidence (dalil) and the rational assessment of ease (yusr).

Core Idea
Qasr salah is pure dropping because the Prophet called it charity; fasting is postponement, not dropping, because ease is ambiguous.

This passage from al-Pazdawi's Usul al-Fiqh tackles a key Hanafi distinction: when does a concession completely drop an obligation versus merely lighten it with a choice? Using the hadith about qasr, al-Pazdawi demonstrates that the linguistic designation 'sadaqah' indicates pure dropping. He contrasts this with fasting, where the Quranic term 'iddah' implies postponement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of rukhsah
Distinguish between rukhsah isqat (dropping the obligation) and rukhsah takhyir (choice between alternatives). Note that isqat means the original ruling no longer applies, while takhyir involves choosing a lighter option.
In Hanafi usul, isqat is a complete lifting; takhyir is a concession that offers a beneficial choice.
2
Analyze the dalil for qasr
Examine the hadith of Umar where the Prophet (saw) called qasr a 'sadaqah'. Understand why 'sadaqah' of something not ownable (like an obligation) implies pure dropping (isqat), not substitution or choice.
The operative phrase is 'صدقة' (sadaqah).
3
Evaluate the rational arguments (ma'na)
Consider two rational arguments: (1) Ease is certain in qasr, so completing prayer is mere burden with no extra reward—thus obligation is dropped. (2) If a choice offers no benefit to the servant, it becomes a divine prerogative (rububiyyah); since qasr offers pure ease, no genuine choice exists.
These arguments reinforce the evidential conclusion.
4
Compare with fasting
Contrast with fasting during travel: the Quran says 'iddah min ayyamin ukhar' (a number of other days), indicating postponement (ta'khir), not dropping. Ease in fasting is ambiguous due to conflicting factors (travel hardship vs. community participation, postponement difficulty vs. comfort of settled life). Thus true choice exists, and fasting is better as the original obligation.
The linguistic evidence (Quran) and ambiguous ease make fasting takhyir, not isqat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك المكره على شرب الخمر، أو أكل الميتة، أو المضطر إليهما رخصة مجازاً؛ لأن الحرمة ساقطة، حتى إذا صبر: صار آثماً؛ لأن حرمته ما ثبتت إلا صيانةً لعقله ودينه عن فساد الخمر، ونفسه عن الميتة، فإذا خاف به فوات نفسه: لم تستقم صيانةُ البعض بفوات الكل، فسقط المحرم، وكان هذا إسقاطاً لحرمته، فإذا صبر: لم يصر مؤدياً حق الله تعالى، فكان مضيعاً دمه، إلا أن حرمة هذه الأشياء مشروعة في الجملة. - ومن ذلك: ما قلنا في قصر الصلاة بالسفر: إنه رخصة إسقاط، حتى لا يصح أداؤه من المسافر، وإنما جعلناها إسقاطاً؛ استدلالاً بدليل الرخصة، ومعناها. أما الدليل: فما روي «أن عمر رضي الله عنه قال: أنقصر الصلاة ونحن آمنون؟ فقال النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام: إن هذه صدقة تصدق الله بها عليكم، فاقبلوا صدقته»(۱). سماه صدقةً، والتصدق بما لا يحتمل التمليك: إسقاط محض، لا يحتمل الرد، وإن كان المتصدق مَن لا تلزم طاعته، كولي القصاص إذا عفا، فمِمَّن تلزم طاعته: أولى. (۱) حديث عمر: أنقصر الصلاة ونحن آمنون ... [بياض في الأصل]... عن يعلى بن أمية قال: قلت لعمر بن الخطاب: ﴿فَلَيْسَ عَلَيْكُمْ جُنَاحٌ أَن نَّقْصُرُوا مِنَ الصَّلَوَةِ إِنْ خِفْتُمْ أَن يَفْتِنَكُمُ الَّذِينَ كَفَرُوا﴾ فقد أَمِنَ الناس؟ فقال: عجبتُ مما عجبت منه، فسألتُ النبيَّ صلى الله عليه وسلم عن ذلك، فقال: صدقة تصدق الله بها عليكم فاقبلوا صدقته». رواه الجماعة إلا البخاري. وأما المعنى فوجهان: أحدهما: أن الرخصة للتيسير، وقد تعيَّن اليُسر في القصر بيقين، فلا يبقى الإكمال إلا مؤنة محضةً، ليس فيها فضلُ ثواب؛ لأن الثواب في أداء ما عليه، فالقصر مع مؤنة السفر: مثلُ الإكمال، كقصر الجمعة مع إكمال الظهر، فوجب القول بالسقوط أصلاً. والثاني: أن التخيير إذا لم يتضمن رفقاً بالعبد: كان ربوبيةً، وإنما للعباد اختيار الأرفق، فإذا لم يتضمن رفقاً بالعبد: كان ربوبية، ولا شركة له فيها. ألا يُرى أن الشرع تولى وضعَ الشرائع جبراً، بخلاف التخيير في أنواع الكفارة ونحوها، لأنه يختار الأرفق عنده. ولهذا لم تُجعل رخصة الصوم إسقاطاً؛ لأن النص جاء بالتأخير بقوله تعالى: ﴿فَعِدَّةٌ مِّنْ أَيَّامٍ أُخَرَ﴾ البقرة/184، لا بالصدقة بالصوم. وإنما إسقاط البعض في هذا نظيرُ التأخير، والحكم هو التأخير، واليُسر فيه متعارض؛ لأن الصوم في السفر يشق عليه من وجه؛ لسبب السفر، ويخِفٌ عليه من وجه بشركة المسلمين، وهي من أسباب اليسر، والتأخيرُ إلى أيام الإقامة يتعذر من وجه وهو الانفراد، ويخف من وجه، وهو الرفق بمرافق الإقامة. والناس في الاختيار متفاوتون، فصار التخيير لطلب الرفق، فصار الاختيار ضرورياً، وللعبد اختيار ضروري، فأما مطلق الاختيار: فلا؛ لأنه إلهى، وصار الصوم أولى؛ لأنه أصل، وقد يشتمل على معنى الرخصة؛
And similarly, the one coerced to drink wine or eat carrion, or the one compelled to them, is a concession metaphorically; because the prohibition is dropped, such that if he endures, he becomes sinful; because its prohibition was only established to safeguard his intellect and religion from the corruption of wine, and his soul from carrion, so if he fears losing his life, safeguarding a part by losing the whole is not sound, so the prohibited thing falls, and this is a dropping of its prohibition, so if he endures, he does not become one who fulfills Allah's right, but becomes one who wastes his life, except that the prohibition of these things is legislated in principle. - And from that is what we said about shortening prayer during travel: that it is a concession of dropping, such that its performance is not valid from the traveler, and we only made it a dropping based on the evidence of the concession and its meaning. As for the evidence: it is narrated that Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) said: 'Do we shorten prayer while we are secure?' The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'This is a charity that Allah has given to you, so accept His charity.' He called it charity, and giving charity of something that does not admit ownership is pure dropping, not subject to rejection, and if the giver is one whose obedience is not obligatory, like the guardian of retaliation if he pardons, then from one whose obedience is obligatory, it is more so. As for the meaning, there are two aspects: one: that the concession is for ease, and ease has been determined in shortening with certainty, so completion remains only pure burden, with no extra reward; because reward is for performing what is due, so shortening with the hardship of travel is like completion, like shortening Friday with completing Zuhr, so the ruling of dropping is necessary absolutely. And the second: that choice, if it does not involve benefit for the servant, would be divinity; and the servants only have the choice of what is more beneficial, so if it does not involve benefit for the servant, it would be divinity, and they have no share in it. Do you not see that the Lawgiver has imposed the laws forcefully, unlike choice in types of expiation and the like, because he chooses what is more beneficial for himself. For this reason, the concession of fasting was not made a dropping; because the text came with postponement in His saying: {Then a number of other days} [Al-Baqarah 2:184], not with charity concerning fasting. And the dropping of part in this is like postponement, and the ruling is postponement, and the ease in it is conflicting; because fasting during travel is difficult from one aspect due to the reason of travel, and it becomes light from one aspect by the participation of Muslims, which is one of the causes of ease, and postponement to days of residence becomes difficult from one aspect, which is being alone, and becomes light from one aspect, which is comfort in the facilities of residence. And people differ in choice, so choice became for seeking ease, thus choice became necessary, and the servant has necessary choice, but absolute choice is not; because it is divine, and fasting is better because it is the original, and it may include the meaning of concession.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage from al-Pazdawi's Usul al-Fiqh discusses the nature of rukhsah (concession) in the Hanafi school, specifically whether certain concessions are 'rukhsah isqat' (a concession that drops the obligation entirely) or merely postponement. The author begins with examples of coercion (ikrah) and necessity (idtarar) regarding wine and carrion. He clarifies that the prohibition is dropped because the original wisdom (safeguarding life and intellect) cannot be achieved by losing the whole. However, if the person endures the hardship without necessity, he becomes sinful. This sets the stage for the main discussion: qasr al-salah (shortening prayer) during travel. The author argues that qasr is a rukhsah isqat based on both evidence and meaning. The evidence is the hadith where the Prophet (saw) called it a 'sadaqah' (charity). Charity that does not involve ownership is pure dropping (isqat). The author also provides rational arguments: (1) If ease is certain in qasr, completing the prayer becomes mere burden with no extra reward, so dropping is necessary. (2) If choice does not involve benefit for the servant, it becomes a divine prerogative; servants can only choose what benefits them. Since qasr is pure ease, there is no real choice. In contrast, fasting during travel was not made a rukhsah isqat because the Quranic text uses the word 'iddah min ayyamin ukhar' (a number of other days), indicating postponement (ta'khir), not dropping. The ease in fasting is ambiguous; there are conflicting factors: difficulty of travel vs. ease of community participation, and postponement has its own difficulties vs. ease of settled life. Thus, choice is genuine for the servant, but absolute choice (like God's) is not. The conclusion is that fasting is better as the original obligation, though it can also have a concessional aspect.

Key Terms
رخصة (rukhsah): A dispensation that allows an act that would otherwise be prohibited or modifies an obligation due to a legitimate excuse.
إسقاط (isqat): The complete dropping of an obligation or prohibition such that the act is no longer required or forbidden.
قصر (qasr): Shortening the four-rak'ah prayers to two rak'ahs during travel.
تخيير (takhyir): Giving a choice between two alternatives, as in types of expiation, where the mukallaf chooses the more beneficial option.
ربوبية (rububiyyah): The exclusive right of God to impose laws without giving the servant a beneficial choice.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is qasr al-salah considered a rukhsah isqat (dropping) and not a rukhsah takhyir (choice) according to al-Pazdawi?
A) Because the hadith calls it a sadaqah, which implies pure dropping
B) Because the traveler can choose to complete the prayer if he wishes
C) Because fasting is also a rukhsah isqat
D) Because the Prophet forbade shortening prayer during travel

Key Takeaway
Qasr al-salah is a rukhsah isqat because the hadith calls it sadaqah, which implies pure dropping; fasting is postponement, not dropping, due to ambiguous ease and Quranic wording.

Does a Command or Prohibition Affect Its Opposite?
Dispute on command/prohibition ruling on opposites

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the three scholarly positions on whether a command or prohibition has a ruling on its opposite, and identify the Hanafi preferred view with its basis in necessity (darurah).

Core Idea
A command does not directly prohibit its opposite but necessitates its dislike; a prohibition may imply its opposite by necessity but not as a primary command.

This passage from Usul al-Bazdawi presents a classic usul dispute: when the Lawgiver commands an act or prohibits it, does that ruling also apply to the opposite of that act? Three views are debated: one denies any effect, al-Jassas asserts a necessary implication, and the Hanafi school adopts a middle path of iqtida' (implication by necessity) leading to dislike (karahah) for the opposite of a command, and a possible recommended sunna for the opposite of a prohibition. Understanding this distinction is crucial for deriving rulings correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
The core question is whether a command or prohibition has any legal ruling (hukm) regarding the opposite action (didd) when that opposite is not explicitly mentioned. Different answers lead to different fiqh consequences.
Focus on the phrase 'هَل لَهُ حُكمٌ في ضِدِّهِ'.
2
Map the three positions
First group: no ruling at all (sākit 'an ghayrihī, silence cannot be a proof). Second group (al-Jassas): command necessitates prohibition of the opposite; prohibition necessitates command of a single opposite. Third group (the chosen Hanafi view): command necessitates dislike (karāha) of the opposite; prohibition may imply its opposite as a necessary but non-primary requirement (iqtidā').
Each position is supported by distinct proofs.
3
Examine the proofs
First group argues from the silence of the text (sukūt). Al-Jassas argues from necessity (darūra): the commanded act cannot coexist with any opposite, so the opposite is implicitly prohibited. He supports this with two ijma examples: the prohibition of concealing menstruation implies a command to disclose (single opposite), and the prohibition on muhrim's stitched garments does not imply a command to wear a specific unstitched garment (multiple opposites). The third group agrees with al-Jassas but modifies the conclusion: the implication is only the minimum, not a full parallel ruling.
Note the two ijma examples in the Arabic: woman and muhrim.
4
Apply the Hanafi tarjih
The author declares the third view 'asahh'indanā' (most correct in our school). Command: the opposite is disliked (makrūh), not prohibited. Prohibition: if it has a single opposite, it may be implied as a sunna wājiba (required sunna); if multiple, no specific opposite is commanded. This is based on the principle that necessity-based implications are secondary, not primary (lā yusāwī al-maqsūd bi-nafsihī).
The key term here is 'iqtiḍā'' — implication by necessity, not by explicit wording.
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical examples: if someone is commanded to stand, sitting or lying down are disliked but not forbidden. A muhrim prohibited from stitched garments is not commanded to wear a specific unstitched garment (e.g., a specific type of ihram cloth) but may wear any permissible covering. This prevents overextending the ruling.
Apply to any command or prohibition in the Shariah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب حكم الأمر والنهي في أضدادهما
واختلف العلماء في الأمر بالشيء: هل له حكم في ضده إذا لم يُقصد ضده بنهي؟
قال بعضُهم : لا حكم له فيه أصلاً.
وقال الجصاص رحمه الله : يوجب ضده إن كان له ضد النهي عن واحد، أو أضداد كثيرة.
وقال بعضهم : يوجب كراهية ضده.
وقال بعضُهم : يقتضي كراهية ضده، وهذا أصح عندنا.
وأما النهي عن الشيء : فهل له حكم في ضده؟ فعلى هذا أيضاً قال الفريق الأول: لا حكم له فيه بوجه.
وقال الجصاص رحمه الله : إن كان له ضد واحد : كان أمراً به، وإن كان له أضداد: لم يكن أمراً بشيء منها.
وقال بعضُهم : يوجب أن يكون ضده في معنى سُنَّةِ واجبة. وعلى القول المختار: يَحتمل أن يقتضي ذلك.
احتج الفريق الأول : بأن كل واحدٍ من القسمين ساكت عن غيره، وقد بينا أن السكوت لا يصلح دليلاً شرعياً ، ألا يُرى أنه لا يصلح دليلاً لما وضع له فيما لم يتناوله إلا بطريق التعليل، فلغير ما وضع له أولى.
واحتج الجصاص رحمه الله : بأن الأمر بالشيء وضع لوجوده، ولا وجود له مع الاشتغال بشيء من أضداده، فصار ذلك من ضرورات حكمه.
وأما النهي : فإنه للتحريم. ومن ضرورته : فعل ضده إذا كان له ضد واحد كالحركة والسكون فأما إذا تعدد الضد : فليس من ضرورة الكف عنه : إتيان كل أضداده، ألا يرى أن المأمور بالقيام إذا قعد، أو نام، أو اضطجع : فقد فوت المأمور والمنهي عن القيام : لا يُفوِّتُ حكم النهي، بأن يقعد، أو ينام، أو يضطجع.
29
قال: وأجمع الفقهاء رحمهم الله أن المرأة منهية عن كتمان الحيض بقوله تعالى: ﴿وَلَا يَحِلُّ لَهُنَّ أَن يَكْتُمْنَ مَا خَلَقَ اللَّهُ فِي أَرْحَامِهِنَّ ﴾ البقرة / ۲۲۸ ، ثم كان ذلك أمراً بالإظهار ؛ لأن الكتمان ضده واحد، وهو الإظهار. واتفقوا أن المُحرِمَ منهي عن لبس المخيط، ولم يكن مأموراً بلبس شيء متعين من غير المخيط.
واحتج الفريق الثالث : بأن الأمر على ما قال الجصاص رحمه الله ، إلا أنَّا أثبتنا بكل واحد من القسمين أدنى ما يثبت به؛ لأن الثابت لغيره ضرورةً : لا يساوي المقصود بنفسه.
وأما الذي اخترناه فبناءً على هذا وهو أن هذا لما كان أمراً ضرورياً سميناه اقتضاء، ومعنى الاقتضاء ها هنا: أنه ضروري غير مقصود، فصار شبيهاً بما ذكرنا من مقتضيات أحكام الشرع.
Chapter: The ruling of command and prohibition regarding their opposites. The scholars differed concerning commanding something: Does it have a ruling regarding its opposite when the opposite is not intended by a prohibition? Some said: It has no ruling regarding it at all. Al-Jassas, may Allah have mercy on him, said: It necessitates its opposite if it has one opposite; [if] it has many opposites, [it necessitates] prohibition of one or many. Some said: It necessitates dislike of its opposite. Some said: It necessitates dislike of its opposite, and this is more correct in our view. As for prohibiting something: Does it have a ruling regarding its opposite? Likewise, the first group said: It has no ruling regarding it in any respect. Al-Jassas, may Allah have mercy on him, said: If it has one opposite, it is a command for it; if it has many opposites, it is not a command for any of them. Some said: It necessitates that its opposite be in the meaning of a required sunna. According to the chosen view, it may imply that. The first group argued: Each of the two categories is silent about the other, and we have shown that silence is not valid as a legal proof. Do you not see that it is not valid as a proof for what it is established for regarding what it does not cover except by way of reasoning, so for other than what it is established for it is even more unsuitable. Al-Jassas, may Allah have mercy on him, argued: Commanding something is established for its existence, and it cannot exist while occupied with any of its opposites, so that becomes a necessity of its ruling. As for prohibition, it is for prohibition. And it is a necessity of it to do its opposite if it has a single opposite, like movement and stillness. But if the opposite is multiple, then it is not a necessity of refraining from it to perform all its opposites. Do you not see that the one commanded to stand, if he sits, sleeps, or lies down, has missed the commanded act; and the one prohibited from standing does not nullify the ruling of prohibition by sitting, sleeping, or lying down. He said: The jurists, may Allah have mercy on them, are agreed that a woman is prohibited from concealing menstruation by His statement, the Exalted: 'And it is not lawful for them to conceal what Allah has created in their wombs' (al-Baqarah 2:228). Then that was a command to disclose, because concealment has only one opposite, which is disclosure. And they agreed that the muhrim is prohibited from wearing stitched garments, and he is not commanded to wear anything specific from unstitched garments. The third group argued: The command is as al-Jassas said, may Allah have mercy on him, except that we establish by each of the two categories the least that can be established by it, because what is established by way of necessity for something else does not equal what is intended by itself. As for what we have chosen, it is based on this: that since this was a necessary matter, we called it 'implication' (iqtida'), and the meaning of implication here is that it is necessary but not intended, so it became similar to what we mentioned of the implications of legal rulings.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage is a usul al-fiqh dispute about whether a command or prohibition has any legal effect on the opposite of the commanded or prohibited act. The author presents three positions. First, some scholars hold that command has no ruling on its opposite at all, and similarly prohibition has no ruling on its opposite. Their proof is that the command is silent about the opposite, and silence cannot be a legal proof. Second, al-Jassas argues that a command necessarily implies prohibition of its opposite(s) because the commanded act cannot exist while engaging in any opposite. For example, if you are commanded to stand, you cannot simultaneously sit, so the command implicitly forbids sitting. For prohibition, if it has a single opposite, the prohibition implies a command to do that opposite (e.g., prohibition of stillness implies command of movement). If there are multiple opposites, the prohibition does not command any specific one. Al-Jassas supports this with two examples of scholarly consensus: (1) The prohibition on women concealing menstruation (Qur'an 2:228) is understood as a command to disclose, since concealment has only one opposite. (2) The prohibition of a muhrim wearing stitched garments does not command him to wear any specific unstitched garment, since there are many unstitched options. Third, another group agrees with al-Jassas but adds that the implication is only minimal, because it is by necessity and not the primary intent. The author declares the third view (that command necessitates dislike of its opposite and prohibition may imply its opposite in the sense of a recommended sunna) as the most correct in the Hanafi school. The key concept is 'iqtida'' (implication) as a necessary but non-major intent derived from the ruling.

Key Terms
الأمر (al-amr): A command; speech requiring an action
النهي (al-nahy): A prohibition; speech requiring abstention
الضد (al-didd): The opposite of an action
الاقتضاء (al-iqtida'): Implication by necessity, not by primary intent
الضرورة (al-darura): Necessity; that which is required for the existence or understanding of the ruling

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred Hanafi view in this passage, what does a command regarding its opposite entail?
A) Definitely prohibits the opposite
B) Has no effect on the opposite
C) Necessitates dislike (karahah) of the opposite
D) Necessitates a command to do the opposite

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that a command only necessitates dislike of its opposite, not prohibition; a prohibition with a single opposite may imply a required sunna, but not a primary command.

The Principle of Non-Primary Prohibitions
Explaining the rule that a prohibition not directly intended only invalidates if it prevents the commanded act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that a non-primary prohibition only invalidates if it prevents the commanded act, and distinguish between cases where it does and does not cause the act to be missed.

Core Idea
A prohibition not directly intended invalidates only if it prevents the commanded act; otherwise it is merely disliked.

This lesson unpacks a foundational Hanafi usul rule concerning prohibitions that are not the primary object of a command. The passage explains that violating such a prohibition only matters legally if it causes the commanded act to be missed; otherwise it is makruh but not invalidating. We will work through the textual evidence and the diverse applications that illustrate this principle, including an internal difference between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core rule
State the principle: when a prohibition is not the primary intent of a command, it only invalidates if it is mufawwit (causes the commanded act to be missed).
Memorize this axiom as the key takeaway.
2
Analyze the textual evidence
Examine Quran 2:228 and the hadith 'لا نكاح إلا بشهود' to see how they illustrate non-primary prohibitions. Both texts appear prohibitive but are actually commands by intermediary (e.g., abrogation or condition).
Note the distinction between a true nahy and a negative used to convey a command.
3
Apply to prayer examples
Trace how the rule applies to the command to stand (sitting does not prevent standing) and to sujood on najis (debate).
The difference turns on whether the act prevents the commanded obligation.
4
Contrast with primary prohibitions
Understand why fasting is different: the prohibition of eating is the primary intent of the command to abstain, so eating always invalidates fasting.
Primary prohibitions are always mufawwit by their nature.
5
Evaluate the intra-madhhab dispute
Compare Abu Yusuf's position (non-primary does not invalidate) with Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (it does invalidate due to continuity of the obligation).
The mufta bihi is the view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَلَا يَحِلُّ هُنَّ أَن يَكْتُمْنَ) البقرة/ ٢٢٨ : فليس بنهي، بل نسخ له أصلاً، مثل قوله تعالى: (لَا يَحِلُّ لَكَ النِّسَاءُ مِنْ بَعْدُ الأحزاب / ٥٢ ، فلا يصير الأمر ثابتاً بالنهي، بل لأن الكتمان لم يبق مشروعاً؛ لما تعلَّق بإظهاره من أحكام الشرع، فصار بهذه الواسطة أمراً. وهذا مثل قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: «لا نكاح إلا بشهود». وفائدة هذا الأصل: أن التحريم إذا لم يكن مقصوداً بالأمر : لم يُعتبر إلا من حيث يُفوِّت الأمر، فإذا لم يُفوِّتُه : كان مكروهاً، كالأمر بالقيام: ليس بنهي عن القعود قصداً، حتى إذا قعد، ثم قام: لم تفسد صلاته بنفس القعود، ولكنه يكره. ولهذا قلنا: إن المُحرِم لما نهي عن لبس المخيط : كان من السنة لُبس الإزار والرداء. ولهذا قلنا: إن العدة لما كان معناها النهي عن الخروج والتزوج : يكن الأمر بالكف مقصوداً، حتى انقضت الأعداد منها بزمان واحد. بخلاف الصوم؛ لأن الكف وجب بالأمر مقصوداً به. ولهذا قال أبو يوسف رحمه الله : إن من سجد على مكان نجس لم تفسد صلاته؛ لأنه غير مقصود بالنهي، وإنما المقصود بالأمر فعل السجود على مكان طاهر، وهذا لا يوجب فواته حتى إذا أعادها على مكان طاهر: جاز عنده. ولهذا قال أبو يوسف رحمه الله : إن إحرام الصلاة لا ينقطع بترك القراءة في مسائل النفل ؛ لأنه أمر بالقراءة، ولم ينه عن تركها قصداً، فصار الترك حراماً بقدر ما يَفُوت من الفرض، وذلك بهذا الشفع، فأما احتمال شفع آخر فلا ينقطع به. ولا يلزم أن الصوم يبطل بالأكل ؛ لأن ذلك الفرض ممتد، فكان ضده مفوتاً أبداً . ولهذا قلنا : إن السجود على مكان نجس : يقطع الصلاة عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد رحمهما الله، وهو ظاهر الجواب ؛ لأن السجود لما كان فرضاً: صار الساجد على النجس مستعملاً ،له بمنزلة الحامل له بحكم الفرضية والتطهير عن حمل النجاسة فرض دائم في أركان الصلاة، وفي المكان أيضاً ، فيصير ضده مفوتاً للفرض. ولهذا قال محمد رحمه الله : إن إحرام الصلاة ينقطع بترك القراءة في النفل في ركعة؛ لأن القراءة فرض دائم في التقدير حكماً على ما عُرف، فينقطع الإحرام بانقطاعه بمنزلة أداء الركن مع النجاسة.
As for His saying, exalted is He, 'And it is not lawful for them to conceal' [Baqarah 2:228], it is not a prohibition, rather it is abrogated originally, like His saying, exalted is He, 'It is not lawful for you [to have more] women after this' [Ahzab 33:52]. Thus the command is not established by the prohibition, but because concealment is no longer prescribed due to the rulings of the Law that are attached to its disclosure, so it becomes a command by this intermediary. And this is like his saying, peace be upon him: 'There is no marriage except with witnesses.' And the benefit of this principle is that when a prohibition is not intended by the command, it is only considered in terms of whether it causes the commanded act to be missed; if it does not cause it to be missed, it is disliked. Like the command to stand: it is not a prohibition of sitting by intent, so if one sits and then stands, his prayer is not invalidated by the sitting itself, but it is disliked. And for this we said: when the muhrim is prohibited from wearing sewn garments, it is sunnah to wear the izaar and rida'. And for this we said: when the 'iddah's meaning is the prohibition of leaving and marrying, the command to stay is not intended, so the 'iddah periods expire by one time. Unlike fasting, because the restraint is obligated by the command intended thereby. And for this Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him, said: if one prostrates on a najis place, his prayer is not invalidated, because it is not the intended object of the prohibition; rather the intended object of the command is the act of prostration on a pure place, and this does not necessitate its being missed, so if he repeats it on a pure place, it is permissible according to him. And for this Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him, said: the ihram of prayer is not broken by leaving the recitation in matters of supererogatory prayer, because he was commanded to recite and was not prohibited from leaving it by intent, so the leaving becomes prohibited to the extent that the obligatory act is missed, and that is in this set, but as for the possibility of another set, it is not broken by it. And it does not follow that fasting is invalidated by eating, because that obligation is continuous, so its opposite is always causing it to be missed. And for this we said: prostration on a najis place breaks the prayer according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on them, and that is the apparent answer, because when prostration is an obligation, the one prostrating on the najis is using it, and it is like the carrier of it by virtue of the obligatory nature, and purification from carrying najasah is a continuous obligation in the pillars of prayer and also in the place, so its opposite causes the obligation to be missed. And for this Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, said: the ihram of prayer is broken by leaving the recitation in supererogatory prayer in one rak'ah, because recitation is a continuous obligation by estimation as a legal ruling as is known, so the ihram is broken by its cessation, like performing a pillar with impurity.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage presents a fundamental Hanafi usul principle: when a prohibition is not the primary intent of a command, violating that prohibition only matters legally if it prevents the commanded act; otherwise it is merely disliked. The author begins by analyzing a Qur'anic verse (Baqarah 2:228) that appears to prohibit concealment, but explains it is not a true prohibition but rather abrogated—the earlier ruling of concealment is no longer prescribed. Similarly, the hadith 'No marriage except with witnesses' is cited, implying that the prohibition of marriage without witnesses is not primary; the command is to have witnesses, and the absence of witnesses prevents the commanded valid marriage. The principle is then applied to several fiqh issues: (1) In prayer, the command to stand does not prohibit sitting per se; if one sits and then stands, the prayer is not invalid but the sitting is disliked because it does not prevent the commanded standing. (2) For a muhrim, the prohibition of sewn garments means it is sunnah to wear the izaar and rida', as the command is to avoid sewn garments, and wearing unsewn garments fulfills the command. (3) In 'iddah, the prohibition of leaving and marrying means the command to remain is not primary; the 'iddah ends after a single period. (4) Fasting is different because the command to abstain is primary, so eating invalidates fasting. (5–6) Abu Yusuf applies the principle to prayer: prostration on a najis place does not invalidate prayer because the prohibition of najasah is not primary; one can repeat on a pure place. Similarly, leaving recitation in nafl prayer does not break the ihram of prayer because the command to recite is not primary; the duty is fulfilled by the set. However, (7–8) Abu Hanifa and Muhammad disagree on these applications: they argue that since prostration is an obligation, and purification from najasah is a continuous obligation, performing sujood on impurity prevents fulfilling the obligation, thus invalidating prayer. Likewise, Muhammad holds that recitation in nafl is a continuous obligatory estimation, so leaving it breaks the ihram. The passage thus illustrates both the rule and intra-madhhab differences in its application.

Key Terms
مقصود بالأمر (maqsud bi'l-amr): The primary intent of a command; in this context, a prohibition that is directly intended by the legislator as the opposite of the commanded act.
مفوت (mufawwit): That which causes the commanded act to be missed or lost; a violation that prevents fulfillment of the obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the principle explained, if a prohibition is not the primary intent of a command, then violating it only matters if it:
A) is done intentionally
B) prevents fulfilling the commanded act
C) is done repeatedly
D) is done in public

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, a prohibition that is not the primary intent of a command only invalidates the commanded act if it causes that act to be missed; otherwise it is merely disliked.

Maqsud Principle: Intended Command vs. Unintended Prohibition
Usul expansion with maqsud principle

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maqsud principle to distinguish acts whose omission directly violates a command from acts whose omission is merely not prohibited, and derive rulings for cases involving salah and sawm.

Core Idea
An act commanded directly (maqsud bil-amr) breaks the obligation if omitted; a merely prohibited act does not necessarily nullify.

This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul introduces a foundational principle: the distinction between what is directly intended by a command (maqsud bil-amr) and what is merely not prohibited (ghayr maqsud bin-nahi). Understanding this distinction is key to determining when an omission actually invalidates an act of worship. The author uses examples from fasting, prostration on impure ground, and recitation in nafl prayer to illustrate how the imams of the Hanafi school apply this principle, revealing a nuanced approach that balances textual commands with the certainty of proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Maqsud Principle
Locate the core rule: acts directly commanded (maqsud bil-amr) cause invalidation if omitted, while acts only prohibited incidentally (ghayr maqsud bin-nahi) do not necessarily invalidate. Note the contrast between fasting and prostration.
Fasting: abstention (kaff) is directly commanded, so eating breaks it. Prostration on najis: the command is for prostration on pure ground, not a direct prohibition against impurity.
2
Analyze Abu Yusuf's Application to Prostration
Read Abu Yusuf's ruling: prostrating on a najis place does not break prayer because the prohibition of impurity is not the direct object of the command. He allows repeating the prostration on pure ground within the same prayer.
Contrast with Abu Hanifa and Muhammad who rule it breaks prayer due to the continuous obligation of purity.
3
Examine the Ikhtilaf on Recitation in Nafl
Study the three positions on missing recitation in nafl: Abu Yusuf (no break), Muhammad (break in each rak'ah), Abu Hanifa (break only if entire set omitted). Identify the reasoning: continuous obligation (Muhammad) vs. probable proof (Abu Hanifa).
The 'probable proof' (dalil muhtamal) is the hadith 'No prayer except with recitation...' which is ahad.
4
Assess the Role of Certainty in Proof
Note how Abu Hanifa distinguishes between probable (zanni) and certain (qat'i) proofs. A probable proof can establish corruption in one rak'ah but does not extend to break the entire ihram; certain proof does.
This is a key usul principle: rulings affecting the fundamental validity (ihram) require stronger evidence.
5
Synthesize the Principle
The passage concludes that these examples form a principle (asl) with many branches. Summarize: the maqsud principle, combined with the continuity of obligation and strength of evidence, determines whether an omission invalidates an act.
This principle recurs across Hanafi fiqh in rulings on prayer, fasting, hajj, and transaction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بخلاف الصوم؛ لأن الكف وجب بالأمر مقصوداً به.
ولهذا قال أبو يوسف رحمه الله : إن من سجد على مكان نجس لم تفسد صلاته؛ لأنه غير مقصود بالنهي، وإنما المقصود بالأمر فعل السجود على مكان طاهر، وهذا لا يوجب فواته حتى إذا أعادها على مكان طاهر: جاز عنده
ولهذا قال أبو يوسف رحمه الله : إن إحرام الصلاة لا ينقطع بترك القراءة في مسائل النفل ؛ لأنه أمر بالقراءة، ولم ينه عن تركها قصداً، فصار الترك حراماً بقدر ما يَفُوت من الفرض، وذلك بهذا الشفع، فأما احتمال شفع آخر فلا ينقطع به.
ولا يلزم أن الصوم يبطل بالأكل ؛ لأن ذلك الفرض ممتد، فكان ضده مفوتاً أبداً .
ولهذا قلنا : إن السجود على مكان نجس : يقطع الصلاة عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد رحمهما الله، وهو ظاهر الجواب ؛ لأن السجود لما كان فرضاً: صار الساجد على النجس مستعملاً ،له بمنزلة الحامل له بحكم الفرضية والتطهير عن حمل النجاسة فرض دائم في أركان الصلاة، وفي المكان أيضاً ، فيصير ضده مفوتاً للفرض.
ولهذا قال محمد رحمه الله : إن إحرام الصلاة ينقطع بترك القراءة في النفل في ركعة؛ لأن القراءة فرض دائم في التقدير حكماً على ما عُرف، فينقطع الإحرام بانقطاعه بمنزلة أداء الركن مع النجاسة.
وقال أبو حنيفة رحمه الله : الفساد بترك القراءة في ركعة ثابت بدليل محتمل) ، فلم يتعد إلى الإحرام، وإذا تَرَكَ في الشفع كله: فقد صار الفساد مقطوعاً به بدليل يوجِبُ العلم، فتعدى إلى الإحرام.
ولهذا قال: في مسافر ترك القراءة : إن إحرام الصلاة لا ينقطع، وهو قول أبي يوسف رحمه الله ؛ لأن الترك متردّد، محتمل للوجود ؛ لاحتمال نية الإقامة، فلم يصلح مفسداً.
فصار هذا الباب أصلاً يجب ضبطه ، تُبتني عليه فروع يطول تعدادها، والله أعلم بالحقائق.
*
(۱) قوله بدليل محتمل : هو ما أخرجه الطبراني من حديث أبي هريرة رضي الله عنه : نادى منادي رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : لا صلاة إلا بقراءة ولو بفاتحة الكتاب.
Contrary to fasting; because abstention was obligated by command intended thereby. For this reason Abu Yusuf said: Whoever prostrates on a najis place, his prayer is not invalidated; because it is not intended by the prohibition, and the intended object of the command is the act of prostrating on a pure place, and this does not necessitate its omission, so that if he repeats it on a pure place: it is permissible according to him. For this reason Abu Yusuf said: The ihram of prayer is not broken by leaving recitation in nafl matters; because he is commanded to recite, and he is not prohibited from leaving it intentionally, so leaving becomes unlawful only to the extent that it misses the obligatory, and that is by this set [of rak'ahs]; as for the possibility of another set, it is not broken by it. And it does not follow that fasting is invalidated by eating; because that obligation is continuous, so its opposite is forever causing loss. For this reason we said: Prostration on a najis place cuts the prayer according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, and it is the apparent answer; because since prostration is an obligation, the one who prostrates on najis is using it, and it is like carrying it due to the obligatoriness, and purification from carrying najasah is a perpetual obligation in the pillars of prayer and also in the place, so its opposite becomes causing loss of the obligation. For this reason Muhammad said: The ihram of prayer is broken by leaving recitation in nafl in a rak'ah; because recitation is a perpetual obligation in estimation by ruling as is known, so the ihram is broken by its cessation, like performing a pillar with najasah. Abu Hanifa said: Corruption by leaving recitation in one rak'ah is established by a probable proof, so it does not extend to the ihram; but if he leaves it in the entire set, then the corruption is established with certainty by a proof that necessitates knowledge, so it extends to the ihram. For this reason he said regarding a traveler who leaves recitation: The ihram of prayer is not broken, and it is the saying of Abu Yusuf; because the leaving is uncertain, probable of existence due to the possibility of intending residence, so it is not a cause of corruption. So this chapter becomes a principle that must be mastered, upon which are built branches too numerous to count. And Allah knows best the realities. * (1) His saying 'by a probable proof' refers to what al-Tabarani narrated from Abu Hurayra: The caller of the Messenger of Allah called out: 'No prayer except with recitation, even if it is the Fatiha of the Book.'
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage discusses a key legal principle: the distinction between what is directly intended (maqsud) by a command and what is merely not prohibited. The author uses examples from prayer and fasting to illustrate the rule. Starting with fasting: abstention (kaff) is directly commanded, so eating necessarily breaks it. For prayer, the command is to perform actions like prostration on a pure place; the prohibition of prostration on a najis place is not the direct object of the command. Abu Yusuf argues that if one prostrates on a najis spot, the prayer is not automatically invalidated because the prohibition is not intended. However, Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that since prostration is wajib itself, doing it with impurity effectively nullifies the obligation of purity, which is a continuous requirement. Similarly, regarding recitation in nafl prayer: Abu Yusuf says missing it does not break the ihram of prayer because recitation is commanded but leaving it is not specifically prohibited. Muhammad says the ihram breaks because recitation is a perpetual duty in each rak'ah. Abu Hanifa distinguishes between leaving one rak'ah (established by a probable proof) and leaving an entire set (certain proof). The footnote clarifies that the 'probable proof' is a hadith from al-Tabarani. The passage concludes that these examples form a foundational maxim with many applications.

Key Terms
مقصود بالأمر (maqsud bil-amr): The act that is directly intended and commanded; its omission directly contradicts the command.
غير مقصود بالنهي (ghayr maqsud bin-nahi): An act that is prohibited but not as the direct object of the prohibition; rather it is a side effect or implied prohibition.
دليل محتمل (dalil muhtamal): A proof that is open to interpretation or not definitive (zanni), allowing for multiple possible rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why does leaving recitation in one rak'ah of nafl prayer NOT break the ihram completely?
A) Because recitation is not a pillar in nafl.
B) Because the proof for corruption in one rak'ah is probable, not certain.
C) Because recitation is not commanded in nafl.
D) Because one rak'ah is not a complete set.

Key Takeaway
The maqsud principle, combined with continuity of obligation and certainty of proof, determines whether an omission invalidates an act of worship in the Hanafi school.

Obligation Attributed to Causes, Not Address
Establishing obligation through causes, not address

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that obligation is tied to legal causes (asbab) rather than direct address (khitab), and derive rulings for scenarios where the address is absent (e.g., sleep, insanity).

Core Idea
Obligation is based on the cause, not on the presence of the command.

This passage from Imam al-Bazdawi's Usul al-Fiqh clarifies a foundational Hanafi principle: wujub (obligation) is attached to the cause (sabab) placed by the Lawgiver, not to the direct address (khitab). This allows for obligation to exist even when the person is not currently addressed, as in sleep or insanity. The author uses consensus (ijma) as proof and discusses a related dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Principle
Read the first part of the passage. State in one sentence the relationship between obligation and cause according to the author.
The author says: 'إنما الوجوب بإيجاب الله تعالى لا أثر للأسباب في ذلك، وإنما وضعت تيسيراً على العباد.' But then says obligation is attributed to causes for ease.
2
Examine the Proof (Ijma)
Find the consensus statement. Identify which cases are cited (sleeping person, insane, fainted) and how they demonstrate that obligation exists without address.
The consensus is cited as 'إجماعهم على وجوب الصلاة على النائم في وقت الصلاة، والخطاب عنه موضوع.'
3
Understand the Madhhab Dispute
Locate the mention of al-Shafi'i and the examples of 'ushr and sadaqat al-fitr. Explain how the author uses these to reinforce the principle.
Al-Shafi'i holds zakat obligatory on a child; the author points to universal agreement on 'ushr and fitra to show obligation from cause.
4
Define the Legal Cause (Sabab)
From the definition given, list the criteria that indicate a cause (e.g., relation of ruling to it, accompaniment, repetition).
The author says: 'وإنما يُعرف السبب بنسبة الحكم إليه وتعلقه به... وإذا لازمه فتكرر بتكرره.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب بيان أسباب الشرائع

اعلم أن الأمر والنهي على الأقسام التي ذكرناها إنما يُراد بها طلب الأحكام المشروعة وأداؤها، وإنما الخطاب للأداء.
=
ولهذه الأحكام أسباب تضاف إليها شرعية، وضعت تيسيراً على العباد، وإنما الوجوب بإيجاب الله تعالى لا أثر للأسباب في ذلك، وإنما وضعت تيسيراً على العباد.
ولما كان الإيجاب غيباً عنا : نُسب الوجوب إلى الأسباب الموضوعة، وثبت الوجوب جبراً، لا اختيار للعبد فيه.
ثم الخطاب بالأمر والنهي للأداء بمنزلة البيع، يجب به الثمن، ثم يطالب بالأداء.
ودلالة صحة هذا الأصل: إجماعُهم على وجوب الصلاة على النائم
في وقت الصلاة، والخطاب عنه موضوع.
وووجوب الصلاة على المجنون إذا انقطع جنونه دون يوم وعلى المغمى عليه كذلك، والخطاب عنهما موضوع.
وليلة،
وكذلك الجنون إذا لم يستغرق شهر رمضان كلّه.
والإغماء والنوم وإن استغرقه لا يمتنع بهما الوجوب، ولا خطاب عليهما بالإجماع.

وقد قال الشافعي رحمه الله بوجوب الزكاة على الصبي، وهو غير مخاطب بالأداء.
غيرِ
وقالوا جميعاً بوجوب العشر، وصدقة الفطر عليه.
فعلم بهذه الجملة : أن الوجوب في حقنا مضاف إلى أسباب شرعية، لا الخطاب.
وإنما يُعرف السبب بنسبة الحكم إليه وتعلقه به؛ لأن الأصل في إضافة الشيء إلى الشيء أن يكون سبباً له، وحادثاً به، وكذلك إذا لازمه فتكرر بتكرره : دل أنه مضاف إليه.
فإذا ثبتت هذه الجملة : قلنا: وجوبُ الإيمان بالله تعالى كما هو بأسمائه وصفاته مضاف إلى إيجابه في الحقيقة، لكنه منسوب إلى حدث العالم ؛ تيسيراً على العباد، وقطعاً لحجج المعاندين.
وهذا سبب يلازم الوجوب؛ لأنا لا نعني بهذا أن يكون سبباً لوحدانية الله تعالى، وإنما نعني به أنه سبب لوجوب الإيمان الذي هو فعل العباد، ولا وجوب إلا على مَن هو أهل له ولا وجود لمن هو أهله على ما أجرى به سنته إلا والسبب يلازمه ؛ لأن الإنسان المقصود به وغيره ممن يلزمه الإيمان به عالَم بنفسه، سُمِّي عالَماً ؛ لأنه جعل عَلَماً على وجوده ووحدانيته.
ولهذا قلنا: إن إيمان الصبي صحيح وإن لم يكن مخاطباً ولا مأموراً به؛ لأنه مشروع بنفسه، وسببه قائمٌ في حقه دائم؛ لقيام دوام من هو مقصود به، وصحةُ الأداء تُبتنى على كون المؤدَّى مشروعاً بعد قيام سببه ممن هو أهله ، لا على لزوم أدائه، كتعجيل الدين المؤجل.
Chapter on the explanation of causes of legal rulings.

Know that the command and prohibition in the categories we have mentioned are only intended to seek the prescribed rulings and their performance, and the address is for performance.
=
And these rulings have causes attributed to them legally, placed as a facilitation for the servants. And obligation is only by God's command; causes have no effect in that. They are only placed as a facilitation for the servants.
And since the command is unseen from us, obligation is attributed to the placed causes, and obligation is established compulsorily, with no choice for the servant.
Then the address with command and prohibition for performance is like a sale: the price becomes obligatory by it, then he is called to performance.
And the proof of correctness of this principle is their consensus on the obligation of prayer for the sleeping person during prayer time, while the address is removed from him.
And the obligation of prayer for the insane when his insanity ceases less than a day, and for the one who faints likewise, while the address is removed from them.
And similarly insanity if it does not cover the entire month of Ramadan.
And fainting and sleeping, even if they cover it, do not prevent obligation, and there is no address upon them by consensus.

And al-Shafi'i, may God have mercy on him, said: obligation of zakat on the child, while he is not addressed with performance.
And they all said: obligation of tithe and charity of breaking the fast upon him.
So it is known by this sum that obligation in our right is attributed to legal causes, not to the address.
And the cause is only known by the relation of the ruling to it and its attachment to it; because the original in attributing one thing to another is that it is a cause for it, occurring by it. Similarly, if it accompanies it and repeats with its repetition, it indicates that it is attributed to it.
So if this sum is established, we say: the obligation of faith in God Almighty as He is with His names and attributes is attributed to His obligating in reality, but it is attributed to the occurrence of the world, as a facilitation for the servants and cutting off the arguments of the obstinate.
This is a cause that accompanies the obligation; we do not mean by this that it is a cause for the oneness of God, but rather we mean that it is a cause for the obligation of faith which is an act of the servants. And there is no obligation except on one who is qualified for it, and there is no existence for one who is its subject as He has carried on His custom, except that the cause accompanies it. Because the human who is intended and others who are obliged to have faith in him are a world by themselves, named 'world' because he is made a sign for His existence and oneness.
For this reason we say: the faith of a child is valid even if he is not addressed nor commanded with it; because it is prescribed by itself, and its cause is established in his right permanently, due to the permanence of the one who is intended by it. And the validity of performance is based on the fact that the performed act is prescribed after the establishment of its cause from one who is qualified for it, not on the necessity of its performance, like accelerating a delayed debt.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage is a usul al-fiqh exposition on the nature of obligation (wujub) and its relationship to causes (asbab) and address (khitab). The author, Imam al-Bazdawi, begins by stating that commands and prohibitions are directed at seeking the performance of prescribed rulings, and the address is for performance. Then he introduces the key principle: rulings have causes attached to them that are placed by the Lawgiver as a facilitation for the servants. Obligation is solely by God's command; the causes have no intrinsic effect, but since the command is unseen, obligation is attributed to the causes. This attribution is a legal fiction for ease, but obligation is established as a necessary (jabr) matter with no choice for the servant.

The author uses the analogy of a sale: the address for performance is like the sale contract that makes the price obligatory, then the buyer is called to pay. The proof is the consensus (ijma) that prayer is obligatory on a sleeping person during its time even though the address (khitab) is removed from him because he is asleep. Similarly, for an insane person whose insanity ends within less than a day, and for one who faints, the obligation remains even though address is removed. Even if fainting or sleep covers the entire time, obligation does not cease, and there is no address by consensus.

Then the text mentions a madhhab dispute: al-Shafi'i held that zakat is obligatory on a child (who is not addressed with performance). But the Hanafi position is that obligation is attached to causes, not to address. This is supported by the fact that all scholars agree on the obligation of 'ushr (tithe on land) and sadaqat al-fitr on a child. Thus, the existence of obligation despite the absence of address proves that obligation is based on causes.

The definition of a cause (sabab) is then given: it is known by the relation of the ruling to it and its attachment to it; if a certain circumstance accompanies the ruling and repeats with its repetition, it indicates that it is the cause.

Applying this, the obligation of faith (iman) in God is attributed to His command in reality, but it is attributed to the occurrence of the world as a facilitation and to cut off excuses. The world is a sign (alam) pointing to God's existence and oneness, and this sign is a cause that accompanies the obligation of faith. The human being is called 'alam (world) because he is a sign. Therefore, a child's faith is valid even without being commanded, because the cause (the existence of God and the world as sign) is permanently established for him. The validity of performance (sihhat al-ada') depends on the act being prescribed (mashru') after the cause is established in a qualified person, not on the necessity of performance (luzum al-ada'), similar to accelerating a delayed debt.

Key Terms
سبب (sabab): A legal cause placed by the Lawgiver that leads to a ruling; it is known by the relation of the ruling to it and its attachment to it, and if it accompanies the ruling and repeats with its repetition.
خطاب (khitab): The direct address (command or prohibition) to the accountable person for performance of an act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, why is prayer obligatory on a sleeping person even though he is not addressed?
A) Because the address applies to him mentally.
B) Because obligation is attached to the cause (the time of prayer), not to the address.
C) Because the sleeping person can be woken up.
D) Because the consensus is only an opinion.

Key Takeaway
Obligation is attributed to legal causes (asbab) not to direct address (khitab), as proven by consensus on the sleeping person.
