Classification of Rulings for Qiyas
Introducing the classification of rulings and their objects as a prerequisite for qiyas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four categories of rulings based on the predominant right and explain why this classification is necessary for qiyas.

Core Idea
Rulings are classified by whose right is dominant: Allah's or the servant's, affecting qiyas applicability.

Before engaging in analogical reasoning (qiyas), a jurist must understand the rulings themselves (ahkam) and the objects to which rulings attach. This passage introduces a foundational Hanafi classification of rulings into four categories based on whether the right involved belongs purely to Allah, purely to servants, or is mixed with one side predominant. This classification determines the strictness with which qiyas can be applied, as pure acts of worship are resistant to analogical extension.

Guided Path
1
1. Understand the purpose of classification
Recognize that qiyas is only valid after mastering the types of rulings and their objects. This classification is a prerequisite for sound causal reasoning.
The author says: 'The validity of causal reasoning for qiyas is only after knowing this total'.
2
2. Identify the four categories of rulings
Memorize the four types: (1) pure rights of Allah, (2) pure rights of servants, (3) mixed with Allah's right predominant, (4) mixed with the servant's right predominant.
These categories are based on whose interest is dominant in the ruling.
3
3. Recognize the eight types of Allah's rights
Note that the passage begins listing eight types under 'rights of Allah' but only three are given here: pure worship, pure punishments, and restricted punishments.
The remaining five will be detailed later; for now, focus on the principle of categorization.
4
4. Connect classification to qiyas
Understand that pure rights of Allah (e.g., acts of worship) are less open to qiyas because they are based on textual specification, whereas rights of servants allow more flexibility.
This is a key Hanafi principle: ibadat are not subject to qiyas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب معرفة أقسام الأسباب والعلل والشروط. جملة ما يثبت بالحجج التي سبق ذكرها سابقاً على باب القياس: شيئان : الأحكام المشروعة. والثاني : ما تتعلق به الأحكام المشروعة. وإنما يصح التعليل للقياس بعد معرفة هذه الجملة، فألحقناها بهذا الباب؛ لتكون وسيلةً إليه بعد إحكام طرق التعليل. أما الأحكام فأنواع: ۱ - حقوق الله عزّ وجل خالصةً. ٢ - وحقوق العباد خالصةً. ۳- والثالث: ما اجتمع فيه الحقان، وحق الله تعالى غالب. ٤- والرابع : ما اجتمعا وحق العبد فيه غالب. وحقوق الله تعالى ثمانية أنواع : ١ - عبادات خالصة . ٢ - وعقوبات خالصة . ٣ - وعقوبات قاصرة.
Chapter: Knowing the Categories of Causes, Reasons, and Conditions. The total of what is established by the proofs previously mentioned before the chapter on qiyas: two things: the prescribed rulings, and the second: that to which the prescribed rulings are attached. And indeed the validity of causal reasoning for qiyas is only after knowing this total, so we appended it to this chapter to be a means to it after mastering the methods of causal reasoning. As for the rulings, they are types: 1 - rights of Allah Almighty, pure; 2 - rights of servants, pure; 3 - the third: that in which the two rights are combined, and the right of Allah is predominant; 4 - the fourth: that in which they are combined and the right of the servant is predominant. And the rights of Allah Almighty are eight types: 1 - pure worship; 2 - pure punishments; 3 - restricted punishments.
Source pages p. 671

Commentary
This passage is an introductory section to the chapter on causes and conditions in the science of Usul al-Fiqh. The author states that before engaging in qiyas (analogical reasoning), one must understand two things: the rulings themselves (ahkam) and the objects to which rulings attach (mahkum fih). He then explains that ta'lil (causal reasoning) for qiyas is only sound after knowing this classification, so this chapter is placed before the chapter on qiyas. The rulings are divided into four categories based on whose right is predominant: pure rights of Allah, pure rights of servants, mixed with Allah's right predominant, and mixed with the servant's right predominant. This classification is crucial because it determines the strictness or leniency in applying qiyas and other methods. The rights of Allah are then further divided into eight types, though only three are mentioned here: pure worship (ibadat), pure punishments (uqubat), and restricted punishments (uqubat qasira). The remaining types would be detailed later. This foundational schema is unique to Hanafi usul and guides how different rulings are derived and prioritized.

Key Terms
حقوق الله (huquq Allah): Rights that belong exclusively to Allah, such as acts of worship and penal punishments.
حقوق العباد (huquq al-'ibad): Rights that belong to individuals or groups, such as financial claims and personal injuries.
عبادات (ibadat): Pure acts of worship that are solely for the sake of Allah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Four: pure Allah, pure servant, both with Allah dominant, both with servant dominant

Key Takeaway
Rulings are classified into four types based on whose right is predominant, which determines the extent to which analogical reasoning (qiyas) can be applied.

The Four Categories of Shari'ah Rulings
Classifies Shari'ah rulings into four types by right.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any Shari'ah ruling into one of four categories based on the type of right involved: purely Allah's, purely human, mixed with Allah's right predominant, or mixed with human's right predominant.

Core Idea
Rulings divide into pure divine, pure human, and two mixed types where one right overrides the other.

This lesson introduces the foundational classification of Shari'ah rulings according to the dominant right. The Hanafi school uses this division to determine the degree of flexibility, enforcement, and potential for pardoning or substitution in each ruling. Understanding these categories is essential before applying analogical reasoning (qiyas) to new cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two primary types of rights
Recognize that every ruling involves either the right of Allah (haqq Allah) or the right of human beings (haqq al-'ibad), or both.
These two fundamental rights are the building blocks of the classification.
2
Note the four combinations
Understand that rulings fall into four categories: pure Allah, pure human, mixed with Allah predominant, mixed with human predominant.
The passage enumerates these four explicitly.
3
Apply the predominance criterion
When both rights exist, determine which right outweighs the other based on the nature of the ruling and its consequences.
For example, in theft, Allah's right (hadd) predominates over the victim's right to restitution.
4
Memorize examples
Attach typical examples to each category: prayer (pure Allah), debts (pure human), theft penalty (mixed Allah predominant), qisas (mixed human predominant).
These examples help anchor the categories in concrete rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب معرفة أقسام الأسباب والعلل والشروط
وجملة ما يثبت بالحجج التي سبق ذكرها سابقاً على باب القياس: شيئان: الأحكام المشروعة. والثاني: ما تتعلق به الأحكام المشروعة. وإنما يصح التعليل للقياس بعد معرفة هذه الجملة، فألحقناها بهذا الباب؛ لتكون وسيلةً إليه بعد إحكام طرق التعليل.
أما الأحكام فأنواع:
1 - حقوق الله عزّ وجل خالصةً.
2 - وحقوق العباد خالصةً.
3- والثالث: ما اجتمع فيه الحقان، وحق الله تعالى غالب.
4- والرابع: ما اجتمعا وحق العبد فيه غالب.
وحقوق الله تعالى ثمانية أنواع:
1 - عبادات خالصة.
2 - عقوبات خالصة.
3 - وعقوبات قاصرة.
4 - وحقوق دائرة بين الأمرين.
5 - وعبادة فيها معنى المؤنة.
6 - ومؤنةٌ فيها معنى العبادة.
7 - ومؤنة فيها شبهة العقوبة.
8 - وحق قائم بنفسه.
Chapter on Knowing the Categories of Causes, Effects, and Conditions
And the sum of what is established by the proofs previously mentioned before the chapter on analogical reasoning: two things: legislated rulings. And the second: that to which the legislated rulings are attached. And indeed, reasoning for analogy is valid only after knowing this summary, so we have appended it to this chapter to be a means to it after perfecting the methods of reasoning.
As for the rulings, they are types:
1- Pure rights of Allah Almighty.
2- And pure rights of the servants.
3- And the third: that in which the two rights are combined, and the right of Allah Almighty predominates.
4- And the fourth: that in which they are combined and the right of the servant predominates.
And the rights of Allah Almighty are eight types:
1- Pure acts of worship.
2- Pure punishments.
3- And incomplete punishments.
4- And rights that fluctuate between the two matters.
5- And an act of worship in which there is the meaning of burden.
6- And a burden in which there is the meaning of worship.
7- And a burden in which there is the semblance of punishment.
8- And a right that stands by itself.
Source pages p. 672

Commentary
This passage is a foundational classification of Shari'ah rulings and rights in Hanafi legal theory. The author begins by stating that the chapter addresses the divisions of causes, effects, and conditions. He explains that the proofs previously discussed establish two things: the rulings themselves and the subjects of those rulings. This classification is necessary before analogical reasoning (qiyas) can be properly employed.
The rulings are divided into four categories based on the nature of the right involved: (1) pure rights of Allah, such as acts of worship with no individual benefit; (2) pure rights of servants, such as financial claims; (3) mixed rights where Allah's right predominates, like criminal penalties; (4) mixed rights where the servant's right predominates, like retaliation (qisas).
Then the author details eight types of rights of Allah: pure worship (e.g., prayer), pure punishment (e.g., hadd penalties), incomplete punishments (e.g., discretionary punishments), fluctuating rights (e.g., those combining worship and punishment), worship with a burden aspect (e.g., fasting), burden with worship aspect (e.g., purification), burden resembling punishment (e.g., expiations), and a self-standing right (e.g., the right of Allah in property like zakat threshold). These categories help jurists determine the applicable rules and flexibility in each case.

Key Terms
حكم (hukm): A Shari'ah ruling that is established by the proofs and relates to an action.
حق الله (haqq Allah): A right that belongs to Allah, not waivable by individuals, such as acts of worship and punishments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Two: rights of Allah and rights of servants.
B) Four: pure Allah, pure servant, mixed with Allah predominant, mixed with servant predominant.
C) Eight: based on the eight types of rights of Allah.
D) Three: worship, punishment, and financial.

Key Takeaway
Rulings are classified by the nature of the right involved, and the predominant right determines the flexibility of the ruling.

The Eight Types of Allah's Rights in Hanafi Usul
Classifies Shari'ah rulings and rights into categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list and explain the eight types of rights that belong to Allah according to the Hanafi classification.

Core Idea
Allah's rights span pure worship, pure punishment, and various hybrid forms combining worship, burden, and punishment.

After establishing the four-fold division of rulings, the text delves into the specific types of Allah's rights. These eight categories help jurists understand the nature of obligations and punishments, and how they may be modified or substituted (e.g., expiation instead of punishment). This granular classification is unique to the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the eight categories in order
Use the numbering from the passage: pure worship, pure punishment, incomplete punishment, fluctuating rights, worship with burden, burden with worship, burden resembling punishment, self-standing right.
The list is sequential and each type builds on the previous.
2
Understand the spectrum
Notice the progression from pure worship to pure punishment, with intermediate categories that blend elements.
Types 5-7 show mixing of worship and burden/punishment.
3
Attach examples
For each type, recall a concrete example from fiqh, e.g., prayer (type 1), hadd (type 2), ta'zir (type 3), zakat (type 4), fasting (type 5), purification (type 6), kaffarah (type 7), and the right to zakat threshold (type 8).
These examples are indicative; others may fit.
4
Recognize legal implications
Each type has different rules regarding substitution, compensation, and enforcement, e.g., pure worship cannot be replaced by money, but burden-like types may allow fidya.
This affects fatwa and practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب معرفة أقسام الأسباب والعلل والشروط
وجملة ما يثبت بالحجج التي سبق ذكرها سابقاً على باب القياس: شيئان: الأحكام المشروعة. والثاني: ما تتعلق به الأحكام المشروعة. وإنما يصح التعليل للقياس بعد معرفة هذه الجملة، فألحقناها بهذا الباب؛ لتكون وسيلةً إليه بعد إحكام طرق التعليل.
أما الأحكام فأنواع:
1 - حقوق الله عزّ وجل خالصةً.
2 - وحقوق العباد خالصةً.
3- والثالث: ما اجتمع فيه الحقان، وحق الله تعالى غالب.
4- والرابع: ما اجتمعا وحق العبد فيه غالب.
وحقوق الله تعالى ثمانية أنواع:
1 - عبادات خالصة.
2 - عقوبات خالصة.
3 - وعقوبات قاصرة.
4 - وحقوق دائرة بين الأمرين.
5 - وعبادة فيها معنى المؤنة.
6 - ومؤنةٌ فيها معنى العبادة.
7 - ومؤنة فيها شبهة العقوبة.
8 - وحق قائم بنفسه.
Chapter on Knowing the Categories of Causes, Effects, and Conditions
And the sum of what is established by the proofs previously mentioned before the chapter on analogical reasoning: two things: legislated rulings. And the second: that to which the legislated rulings are attached. And indeed, reasoning for analogy is valid only after knowing this summary, so we have appended it to this chapter to be a means to it after perfecting the methods of reasoning.
As for the rulings, they are types:
1- Pure rights of Allah Almighty.
2- And pure rights of the servants.
3- And the third: that in which the two rights are combined, and the right of Allah Almighty predominates.
4- And the fourth: that in which they are combined and the right of the servant predominates.
And the rights of Allah Almighty are eight types:
1- Pure acts of worship.
2- Pure punishments.
3- And incomplete punishments.
4- And rights that fluctuate between the two matters.
5- And an act of worship in which there is the meaning of burden.
6- And a burden in which there is the meaning of worship.
7- And a burden in which there is the semblance of punishment.
8- And a right that stands by itself.
Source pages p. 672

Commentary
This passage is a foundational classification of Shari'ah rulings and rights in Hanafi legal theory. The author begins by stating that the chapter addresses the divisions of causes, effects, and conditions. He explains that the proofs previously discussed establish two things: the rulings themselves and the subjects of those rulings. This classification is necessary before analogical reasoning (qiyas) can be properly employed.
The rulings are divided into four categories based on the nature of the right involved: (1) pure rights of Allah, such as acts of worship with no individual benefit; (2) pure rights of servants, such as financial claims; (3) mixed rights where Allah's right predominates, like criminal penalties; (4) mixed rights where the servant's right predominates, like retaliation (qisas).
Then the author details eight types of rights of Allah: pure worship (e.g., prayer), pure punishment (e.g., hadd penalties), incomplete punishments (e.g., discretionary punishments), fluctuating rights (e.g., those combining worship and punishment), worship with a burden aspect (e.g., fasting), burden with worship aspect (e.g., purification), burden resembling punishment (e.g., expiations), and a self-standing right (e.g., the right of Allah in property like zakat threshold). These categories help jurists determine the applicable rules and flexibility in each case.

Key Terms
حق الله (haqq Allah): A right that belongs to Allah, not waivable by individuals, such as acts of worship and punishments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Eight
C) Six
D) Ten

Key Takeaway
Allah's rights encompass eight categories ranging from pure worship to pure punishment, with intermediate hybrid forms that affect legal rulings.

Definition of Partial Punishments (Ajziya) and the Nature of Kaffarat
Defining 'ajziya' and distinguishing kaffarat from other punishments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'ajziya' and apply the Hanafi criteria to distinguish kaffarat from complete punishments, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Kaffarat are partial punishments combining worship and penalty, not compensation for damage.

In this passage, al-Bazdawi introduces the category of 'ajziya' — incomplete punishments that are not strictly punitive but also involve worship. He explains how kaffarat (expiations) fit this category, contrasting with complete punishments like hudud. Understanding this categorization is key to many Hanafi rulings, such as when kaffarat are not required from children or indirect causers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of punishments
Locate the classification of punishments into 'complete' (kamila) like hudud and 'incomplete' (qasira) called ajziya. Note the examples: deprivation of inheritance for killing and kaffarat.
The term 'ajziya' is specific to the Hanafi school.
2
Extract the criteria for ajziya
Read carefully: ajziya require taqsir (negligence), are not primary obligations, and combine aspects of worship and punishment. They are recompenses for specific acts, not general obligations.
Taqsir is defined as negligence in matters of Allah's rights.
3
Apply the criteria to examples
Check the examples: deprivation of inheritance applies only to direct killers (mubashir), not indirect causes. Kaffarat are obligatory only on those who are negligent (e.g., intentional breakers, not children or innocent direct causers).
Indirect causers (e.g., witness retracting) are not liable for ajziya at all.
4
Examine the direct vs. indirect causation distinction
Note the key principle: ajziya are penalties for direct causation (mubashara), not indirect (tasabbub). This is analogous to qisas (retaliation) which only applies to the direct killer.
This differentiates ajziya from civil liability where indirect causation may apply.
5
Compare with the Shafi'i view and note practical consequences
Al-Shafi'i treats kaffarat as compensation for damage (daman al-mutlaf). The Hanafi response: kaffarat are from the rights of Allah, not damages; they fall by shubha (doubt) unlike compensation. This leads to differences such as whether a disbeliever owes kaffarat al-fitr.
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference) includes the ruling on kaffarat for disbelievers and the effect of obstacles like menstruation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما العقوبات الكاملة : فمثل الحدود.
وأما القاصرة: فنسميها أجزيةً، مثل حرمان الميراث بالقتل، ولذلك لا تثبت في حق الصبي ؛ لأنه لا يوصف بالتقصير، بخلاف الخاطىء البالغ ؛ لأنه مقصر ، فلزمه الجزاء القاصر ، ولم يلزمه الكامل، والصبي غير مقصر ، فلم يلزمه جزاء القاصر ولا الكامل.
وحافر البئر، وواضع الحجر، والقائد، والسائق، والشاهد إذا رجع : لم يلزمهم الحرمان ؛ لأنه جزاء المباشرة، فلا يجب على صاحب الشرط أبداً، كالقصاص. والحقوق الدائرة هي الكفارات فيها معنى العبادة في الأداء، وفيها معنى العقوبة، حتى لم تجب إلا أجزيةً، ولم تجب مبتدأةً، وجهَةً العبادة فيها غالبة عندنا وهي مع ذلك جزاء الفعل، حتى راعينا فيها صفةَ الفعل، فلم نوجب على قاتل العمد، وصاحب الغموس؛ لأن السبب غير موصوف بشيء من الإباحة.
وقلنا : لا تجب على المسبب الذي قلنا ولا على الصبي؛ لأنها من الأجزية.
والشافعي رحمه الله جعلها ضمان المتلف، وذلك غلط في حقوق الله تعالى، بخلاف الدية.
وكذلك الكفارات كلها ولهذا لم تجب على الكافر ما خلا كفارة الفطر، فإنها عقوبة وجوباً، وعبادة أداءً حتى تسقط بالشبهة على مثال الحدود.
وقلنا : تسقط باعتراض الحيض والمرض، وتسقط بالسفر الحادث بعد الشروع في الصوم إذا اعترض الفطر على السفر، وتسقط بشبهة القضاء، وظاهر السنة فيمن أبصر هلال رمضان وحده ؛ لشبهة في الرؤية.
خلافا للشافعي رحمه الله فإنه ألحقها بسائر الكفارات.
إلا أنا أثبتنا ما قلنا استدلالاً بقول النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم: «مَن أفطر في رمضان متعمداً : فعليه ما على المظاهر» ولإجماعهم على أنها لا تجب على الخاطىء، ولأنا وجدنا الصوم حقاً لله تعالى خالصاً، تدعو الطباع إلى الجناية عليه، فاستدعى زاجراً، لكنه لما لم يكن حقاً مسلماً تاماً : صار قاصراً، فأوجبناه بالوصفين. وقد وجدنا ما يجب عقوبةً، ويُستوفى عبادة، كالحدود.
As for complete punishments: like the hudud. And as for incomplete ones: we call them 'ajziya' (partial recompenses), such as being deprived of inheritance due to killing. Therefore it is not established for a child because he is not described as being negligent, unlike an adult who errs because he is negligent, so the partial recompense is incumbent on him, and the complete is not. The child is not negligent, so neither partial nor complete recompense is incumbent on him. The one who digs a well, and the one who places a stone, and the leader, and the driver, and the witness who retracts: the deprivation is not incumbent on them because it is a recompense for direct causation, so it is not obligatory on the indirect cause ever, like retaliation. The circulating rights are the expiations; they contain the meaning of worship in performance, and they contain the meaning of punishment, so that they are only obligatory as partial recompenses, and not obligatory as a primary obligation, and the aspect of worship is dominant in our view, and despite that they are recompenses for the act, so that we consider the description of the act in them, so we do not make them obligatory on the intentional killer and the one who takes a false oath because the cause is not described with any permissibility. And we said: they are not obligatory on the indirect cause as we said, nor on the child, because they are of the partial recompenses. Al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) made it a compensation for damage, and that is a mistake in matters of the rights of Allah, unlike blood money. Likewise, all expiations, and for this reason they are not obligatory on the disbeliever except for the expiation of breaking fast in Ramadan, for it is a punishment in terms of obligation, and an act of worship in performance, to the extent that it is dropped due to doubt, on the example of hudud. And we said: it is dropped due to the occurrence of menstruation and illness, and it is dropped due to travel that occurs after beginning fasting if breaking fast occurs due to travel, and it is dropped due to doubt about making up, and the apparent sunnah regarding one who sees the crescent of Ramadan alone due to doubt in the sighting. Contrary to al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, for he attached it to the other expiations. But we established what we said by way of inference from the saying of the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'Whoever intentionally breaks fast in Ramadan must do what the one who practices zihar must do,' and by their consensus that it is not obligatory on the one who errs, and because we found fasting to be a pure right of Allah most high, human instincts incline to transgress against it, so it required a deterrent, but since it was not a complete and perfect right, it became incomplete, so we made it obligatory by reason of the two descriptions. And we have found what is obligatory as a punishment and fulfilled as worship, like the hudud.
Source pages p. 676

Commentary
This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul defines a key category of legal obligations: al-ajziya (partial punishments) as distinct from complete punishments (al-uqubat al-kamila). The author explains that ajziya are obligations that combine elements of punishment and worship, but are primarily categorized as recompenses for specific acts. The examples given include deprivation of inheritance for killing, and kaffarat (expiations). The passage emphasizes that these do not apply to children because they lack taqsir (negligence), unlike negligent adults. It also distinguishes between direct causation (mubashara) and indirect causation (tasabbub), stating that indirect causers are not liable for ajziya. A major discussion is the nature of kaffarat: the Hanafis hold that they are partial punishments (ajziya) with a dominant aspect of worship, while al-Shafi'i treats them as compensation for damage (daman al-mutlaf). The Hanafi view is based on a hadith about intentional breaking of fast in Ramadan and on consensus (ijma), and it leads to specific rulings: kaffarat are not required from a disbeliever except for kaffarat al-fitr; they are dropped by obstacles like menstruation, illness, or travel after starting, or doubt about qada. This classification has practical implications as detailed in fiqh.

Key Terms
أجزية (ajziya): العقوبات القاصرة التي تجب جزاءً لفعل معين ولا تجب كحق مبتدأ
كفارة (kaffarah): عبادة وعقوبة معاً، تجب تكفيراً لذنب أو جبراً لخلل في عبادة
تقصير (taqsir): الإهمال أو التفريط في حق الله، وهو شرط لوجوب الأجزية
مباشرة (mubasharah): الفعل المباشر الذي يحدث الضرر، الموجب للجزاء القاصر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Kaffarat in the Hanafi school are partial punishments (ajziya) that combine worship and penalty, require direct causation and negligence, and are dropped by doubt – distinguishing them from complete punishments and civil compensation.

Kaffara as Penalty: Hanafi Usul on Expiation for Breaking Fast
Explains Hanafi view that kaffara for breaking fast is a penalty, not compensation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school classifies kaffara for intentional fast-breaking as a penalty (jaza') rather than compensation (daman), and identify the primary dalil (hadith of zihar), secondary consensus, and analogical reasoning (qiyas to hudud) that support this view.

Core Idea
Kaffara for breaking fast is a penalty that drops with doubt, like hudud, because it is a pure right of Allah and a deterrent.

This passage delves into a foundational usul dispute: is the expiation for intentionally breaking a Ramadan fast a penalty or a compensation? The Hanafi school argues it is a penalty, based on the Prophet's hadith equating it to zihar, consensus, and analogy to hudud. Understanding this classification is essential for grasping when the kaffara falls due to excuses. In this lesson, you will analyze the Hanafi proof structure and contrast it with the Shafi'i compensatory view.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi ruling
Identify the ruling: kaffara for breaking fast is a penalty (jaza'), not compensation, so it drops with doubts and excuses.
2
Examine the primary textual evidence
Analyze the hadith: 'Whoever intentionally breaks fast in Ramadan owes what the one who declares zihar owes.' How does this equate kaffara to a severe penalty?
The hadith is cited as direct proof.
3
Consider the secondary evidences
What is the consensus (ijma) that it does not apply to a mistaken person? What is the qiyas (analogy) to hudud?
Consensus and qiyas reinforce the penal nature.
4
Contrast with the Shafi'i position
How does Shafi'i view it? What is the Hanafi response regarding diya and rights of Allah?
Shafi'i considers it compensatory; Hanafi refutes.
5
Determine the practical consequence
How does the classification affect rulings on menstruation, illness, travel, and shubha?
Penal nature means it drops with excuses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقلنا : لا تجب على المسبب الذي قلنا ولا على الصبي؛ لأنها من الأجزية.
والشافعي رحمه الله جعلها ضمان المتلف، وذلك غلط في حقوق الله تعالى، بخلاف الدية.
وكذلك الكفارات كلها ولهذا لم تجب على الكافر ما خلا كفارة الفطر، فإنها عقوبة وجوباً، وعبادة أداءً حتى تسقط بالشبهة على مثال الحدود.
وقلنا : تسقط باعتراض الحيض والمرض، وتسقط بالسفر الحادث بعد الشروع في الصوم إذا اعترض الفطر على السفر، وتسقط بشبهة القضاء، وظاهر السنة فيمن أبصر هلال رمضان وحده؛ لشبهة في الرؤية.
خلافا للشافعي رحمه الله فإنه ألحقها بسائر الكفارات.
إلا أنا أثبتنا ما قلنا استدلالاً بقول النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم: «مَن أفطر في رمضان متعمداً : فعليه ما على المظاهر»
ولإجماعهم على أنها لا تجب على الخاطىء، ولأنا وجدنا الصوم حقاً الله تعالى خالصاً، تدعو الطباع إلى الجناية عليه، فاستدعى زاجراً، لكنه لما لم يكن حقاً مسلماً تاماً : صار قاصراً، فأوجبناه بالوصفين. وقد وجدنا ما يجب عقوبةً، ويُستوفى عبادة، كالحدود؛ لأن إقامة السلطان عبادةٌ، ولم نجد ما يجب عبادةً ويُستوفى عقوبةً، فصار الأول أولى.
ولهذا قلنا بتداخل الكفارات في الفطر.
We said: It is not obligatory on the one who caused [the fast-breaking] nor on the child, because it is from the penalties. Al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, made it compensation for destroying something, and that is a mistake concerning the rights of Allah, unlike blood-money. Likewise all expiations are like this, and for this reason it is not obligatory on the disbeliever except for the expiation of breaking the fast; for it is a punishment in obligation and an act of worship in performance, so that it drops due to doubt on the model of hudud. We said: It drops due to the occurrence of menstruation and illness, and drops due to travel occurring after starting the fast if breaking the fast occurs due to travel, and drops due to doubt concerning making up, and [based on] the apparent sunna regarding one who sees the Ramadan crescent alone, due to doubt in the sighting. Contrary to al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, for he attached it to other expiations. However, we established what we said as proof by the saying of the Prophet, peace be upon him: 'Whoever intentionally breaks fast in Ramadan owes what the one who declares zihar owes.' And by their consensus that it is not obligatory on the mistaken person, and because we found fasting to be a pure right of Allah, toward which natures impel to violation, so it required a deterrent; but since it is not a complete, perfect right, it is deficient, so we made it obligatory by the two descriptions. And we have found what is obligatory as a punishment and performed as an act of worship, like hudud, because the sultan's enforcement is worship, and we did not find what is obligatory as worship and performed as punishment, so the first is more appropriate. For this reason we said the expiations for breaking fast overlap.
Source pages p. 677

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi usul-fiqh exposition on the nature of kaffara (expiation) for intentionally breaking a fast in Ramadan. The author states the Hanafi ruling that this kaffara is a penalty (jaza') not compensation (daman). Hence it is not obligatory on the one who caused the breaking (e.g., by force-feeding) nor on a child. The Shafi'i position considered it as compensation for destroying something, which the author deems a mistake regarding the rights of Allah, unlike blood-money which is compensatory. All other expiations are similar, except the kaffara for breaking fast is not obligatory on a disbeliever. The ruling details that this kaffara is a punishment in obligation but an act of worship in performance, so it drops due to doubt (shubha) like hudud. The author then lists excuses that cause it to drop: menstruation, illness, travel after starting the fast, and doubt about making up. The proof includes the Prophet's hadith equating the kaffara to that of zihar, and consensus that it is not due from a mistaken person. The reasoning is that fasting is a pure right of Allah, but deficient, so it requires a deterrent, and it shares the nature of hudud: obligatory as punishment, performed as worship. Hence the Hanafi view that kaffara for breaking fast overlaps (tadakhul) when multiple violations occur.

Key Terms
الكفارة (al-kaffara): A penalty or expiation for violating a fast, specifically for intentional breaking of Ramadan fast.
الحدود (al-hudud): Fixed punishments prescribed by Allah for certain crimes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is still obligatory and must be performed.
B) It drops because of the doubt (shubha).
C) It is converted to a monetary payment.
D) It must be performed after the doubt is resolved.

Key Takeaway
Kaffara for intentional fast-breaking is a penalty, not compensation, so it falls with doubts and excuses, distinguishing it from other expiations according to Hanafi usul.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling

Rights of Allah and Servants and the Principle of Asl and Khalaf
Classifies rights and introduces asl/khalaf principle

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify legal rights according to which party's right dominates and apply the Hanafi asl/khalaf principle to derive rulings in ibadah and personal status.

Core Idea
A substitute (khalaf) carries the same legal effect as the original (asl) in Hanafi usul, even if the original is absent.

This passage from Usul al-Bazdawi classifies legal rights and introduces a foundational usul concept: the division of rulings into original (asl) and substitute (khalaf). The author first distinguishes rights based on which party's claim dominates—Allah's right, the servant's right, or both. He then demonstrates how the asl/khalaf framework operates in faith, guardianship, and purification, culminating in the well-known Hanafi position that tayammum is an absolute substitute for water. Understanding this framework will equip you to derive rulings when primary elements are absent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories of rights
The text classifies hadd penalties: hadd qadhf involves both Allah's and servant's rights but Allah's is dominant; qisas is servant-dominant; hadd of highway robbery is purely Allah's. Note the basis for each.
2
Define asl and khalaf conceptually
Asl is the original legal element; khalaf is a substitute that takes its place. The substitute is recognized by shar' and operates under specific conditions.
3
Analyze the application to iman
Original iman is tasdiq (belief) and iqrar (declaration). In dunya rulings, iqrar became an independent original, substituting for tasdiq. This shows how asl can shift.
4
Apply to guardianship and conversion
For a minor, the parent's performance substitutes for the child's own. Similarly, for a minor entering Dar al-Islam without parents, the 'followership' of the people of the abode and soldiers substitutes for parental followership in establishing Islam.
5
Examine the tayammum dispute and Hanafi position
Tayammum is a substitute for water. Hanafi: absolute substitute (khalaf mutlaq), allowing multiple prayers. Shafi'i: only a substitute of necessity, one prayer per tayammum. Identify the thamarah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحقوق العباد أكثر من أن تُحصى.
والمشتمل عليهما وحق الله تعالى غالب: حد القذف.
والذي يغلب فيه حق العبد: القصاص.
فأما حد قطاع الطريق فخالص لله تعالى عندنا، وهذا مما يَطُولُ به الكتاب.
وهذه الحقوق كلها تنقسم إلى أصل وخَلَف، وذلك في الإيمان أولاً، أصله التصديقُ والإقرار على ما فسَّرنا ثم صار الإقرار أصلاً مُستَبداً عن التصديق في أحكام الدنيا، ثم صار أداء أحد الأبوين ثابتاً في حق عن الصغير عن أداء الصغير بنفسه، وكذلك في حق المعتوه والمجنون: لا يُعتبر ذلك، مع ثم صارت تبعية أهل الدار والغانمين خَلَفاً عن تبعية الأبوين في إثبات الإسلام في صغير أدخل دارنا، أو وقع في سهم المسلم إذا لم يكن معه أحد أبويه.
وكذلك في شروط الصلاة: الطهارة بالماء أصل، والتيمم خَلَفٌ، لكن هذا الخلف عندنا مطلق، وعند الشافعي رحمه الله خَلَفٌ ضرورةً، حتى لم يجوز أداء الفرائض بتيمم واحد.
وقال في إناءين نجس وطاهرٍ في السفر : إن التحري فيه جائز، ولم يجعل التراب طهوراً؛ لعدم الضرورة.
And the rights of servants are too many to be counted. And that which includes both and the right of Allah is dominant: the hadd of slander. And that in which the right of the servant is dominant: retaliation. As for the hadd of highway robbers, it is purely for Allah according to us, and this would make the book long. And all these rights are divided into original and substitute. That is first in faith: its original is belief and declaration as we explained; then declaration became an independent original from belief in the rulings of this world. Then the performance of one of the parents became established in the right of the minor as a substitute for the minor's own performance. Likewise regarding the insane and mentally defective: that is not considered. Then the following of the people of the abode and the soldiers became a substitute for the following of the parents in establishing Islam for a minor who enters our abode or falls into the share of a Muslim if neither parent is with him. Likewise in the conditions of prayer: purification with water is the original, and tayammum is a substitute, but this substitute according to us is absolute, while according to al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, it is a substitute of necessity, so he did not permit performing obligatory prayers with one tayammum. And he said regarding two vessels, one pure and one impure, during travel: that ascertaining (taharrī) is permissible, and he did not make earth purifying due to lack of necessity.
Source pages p. 677

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on classification of legal rights. The author notes that the rights of servants are numerous. He gives examples: hadd of qadhf (slander) includes both Allah's right and the servant's right, but Allah's right is dominant. Qisas (retaliation) is dominated by the servant's right. The hadd for highway robbery is purely Allah's right. The author then introduces a key usul concept: division into asl (original) and khalaf (substitute). He applies this to iman (faith): the original is tasdiq (belief in the heart) and iqrar (verbal declaration). In this world's rulings, iqrar became an independent original, separate from tasdiq. Then, for a minor's performance of acts, the parent's performance becomes a substitute for the child's own performance. Similarly, for the insane and mentally deficient, [substitution] is not considered. Then the concept extends: the following/accompaniment of the people of the house (ahl al-dar) and the soldiers (ghanimin) becomes a substitute for parental accompaniment in establishing Islam for a minor who enters dar al-Islam or becomes a captive, if no parent is present. Finally, the principle is applied to conditions of prayer: original purification is with water; tayammum is a substitute. Hanafi holds tayammum is an absolute substitute (khalaf mutlaq), meaning it can be used for any prayer without need for repeated tayammum. Shafi'i holds it is only a substitute of necessity, so one cannot perform multiple obligatory prayers with a single tayammum. Additionally, Shafi'i permits taharrī (ascertaining) regarding two vessels (one pure, one impure) during travel, but does not allow earth as purifier without necessity – here earth refers to tayammum? Actually the text says 'و لم يجعل التراب طهورا' meaning he did not consider earth purifying, i.e., tayammum cannot be used without necessity. The Hanafi view is that tayammum is permissible even without necessity? The text says 'عندنا مطلق' meaning absolute, so in Hanafi, tayammum is a legitimate substitute for water even when water is available? Actually that seems contradictory to normal fiqh. Usually tayammum is only when water is unavailable. But here the text might be contrasting the concept: 'mutlaq' means it fully substitutes water in all rulings, not limited to necessity. The Shafi'i view restricts it to necessity. The example about two vessels and taharrī may illustrate that Shafi'i only allows taharrī (attempt to determine purity) but does not allow tayammum because he considers it a necessity-based substitute, so without necessity one must use water. The Hanafi view is not fully elaborated here but implied.

Key Terms
أصل (asl): The original or primary legal element from which a substitute is derived.
خَلَف (khalaf): A legal substitute that stands in for an original when conditions are met.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Hanafi teaching in this passage, how is tayammum classified relative to purification with water?
A) As a last resort, only when dire necessity exists.
B) As an absolute substitute (khalaf mutlaq) for water.
C) As a partial substitute valid only for supererogatory prayers.
D) As a temporary measure that requires repeating for each prayer.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi usul principle of asl and khalaf allows substitutes to fully bear the legal effect of the original, exemplified by tayammum as an absolute substitute for water.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling

The Principle of Substitution (Khilafah) in Hanafi Usul
Rule statement with detailed talil and intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for substitution (khilafah) and apply it to distinguish cases where the substitute is valid from those where it is not, using the Hanafi usul framework.

Core Idea
Substitution requires the original to be legally possible, even if physically impossible.

This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul articulates a key Hanafi rule: a substitute (khalaf) can only stand in when the original (asl) is absent at the moment but remains legally possible. The rule governs many fiqh cases, including expiation for oaths and liability for blood money. We will explore the principle, its condition, and a detailed intra-madhhab dispute that tests its limits.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle and its source
Locate the statement that substitution is established only by nass or dalalat al-nass. Note the condition: absence of the original with possibility of existence.
The principle is axiomatic in Hanafi usul.
2
Understand the condition through examples
Analyze the yamin ghamus (no substitute because original impossible) versus mas al-sama' (substitute valid because legal possibility exists).
Legal possibility (ihtimal) suffices, not physical feasibility.
3
Trace the intra-Hanafi dispute
Read the case of witnesses of intentional killing when the alleged victim appears alive. Identify the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.
The dispute hinges on whether blood is 'potentially ownable' in law.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response
Examine Abu Hanifa's argument that blood ownership is not legislated, so cause cannot attach, and substitute is invalid. Contrast with the Sahibayn's analogy to mas al-sama'.
The response clarifies the boundary of legal possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا إنما يُستقصى في مبسوط أصحابنا رحمهم الله، وإنما غرضنا الإشارة إلى الأصل، وذلك أن الخلافة لا تثبت إلا بالنص، أو دلالة النص. وشرطه عدم الأصل للحال على احتمال الوجود؛ ليصير السبب منعقداً للأصل، فيصح الخلف، فأما إذا لم يحتمل الأصل الوجود فلا. مثلُ البِرِّ في الغَموس، لما لم يحتمل الوجود: لم تثبت الكفارة خلفاً عنه، بخلاف مسألة مس السماء، وسائر الأبدال، فإنها لم تشرع إلا عند احتمال وجود الأصل. والمسائل على هذا الأصل أكثر من أن تُحصى، وقد سبق بعضها فيمن أسلم في آخر وقت الصلاة. ولهذا قال أبو يوسف ومحمد رحمهما الله: في المشهود بقتله إذا جاء حياً، وقد قتل المشهود عليه، فاختار الولي تضمين الشهود: إنهم يرجعون على الولي؛ لأن سبب الملك قد وجد وهو التعدي والضمان والمضمون وهو الدم محتمل للملك في الشرع، غير مستحيل، مثل مس السماء، فعمل في بدله وهو الدية عند تعذر العمل بالأصل. كما قيل في غاصب المدبر من الغاصب إذا مات المدبر عند الثاني، أو أبق: إن الأول إذا ضمن: رجع به على الثاني وإن لم يملك المدبر. وكذلك شهود الكتابة إذا رجعوا بعد الحكم، وضمنوا قيمته: رجعوا ببدل الكتابة على المكاتب ولم يملكوا رقبته؛ لما قلنا إن سبب الملك قد وجد، والأصل يحتمل الملك، فإذا لم يثبت الملك: قام البدل مقامه. وأما أبو حنيفة رحمه الله فقد قال: إن الشهود متلفون حكماً بطريق التسبيب، والولي متلفٌ حقيقةً بالمباشرة، وهما سواء في حق ضمان الدية، وإذا كان الولي لا يرجع لم يرجع الشهود أيضاً. بخلاف شهود الخطأ، فإنهم إذا ضمنوا وقد جاء المشهود بقتله حياً: رجعوا لأنهم لا يضمنون بالإتلاف، لكن بما أوجبوا للولي، فإذا ضمنوا: صار الولي متلفاً عليهم؛ لأن المضمون ثمَّة المال، وهو محتمل للملك. والجواب عن قولهما: إن ملك الأصل المتلف، وهو الدم غير مشروع أصلاً، ولا يحتمل، فلا ينعقد السبب له فيبطل الخلف، ولأن الخلف يحكي الأصل، والأصل هو الدم المتلف، وملك الدم هو القصاص، والأصل بنفسه غير مضمون لو صار ملكاً، فكذلك خلفه. وفي المدير: الأصل مضمون متى كان ملكاً لا محالة، فكذلك بدله.
And this is thoroughly investigated in the detailed works of our scholars (may Allah have mercy on them), but our purpose is to indicate the principle: that substitution is only established by explicit text (nass) or the indication of a text. Its condition is the absence of the original at present with the possibility of its existence; so that the cause becomes attached to the original, and the substitute is valid. But if the original cannot possibly exist, then not. Like righteousness (al-birr) in the false oath (al-ghamus), since it cannot possibly exist, expiation is not established as a substitute for it. Unlike the issue of 'touching the sky' (mas al-sama') and other substitutes, for they are only legislated when there is possibility of the original's existence. Cases based on this principle are too many to enumerate, and some have preceded regarding one who embraces Islam at the end of prayer time. For this reason, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on them) said: Regarding the one witnessed as killed if he comes alive while the accused has been killed, and the heir (wali) chooses to hold the witnesses liable for blood money: they (the witnesses) can recover from the heir, because the cause of ownership has occurred—that is, transgression and liability—and the subject (blood) is potentially ownable in law, not impossible, like 'touching the sky' (analogy to a possible thing). Thus its substitute—the blood money—operates when the original cannot be implemented. Similarly, it is said regarding the usurper (ghasib) of a mudabbar (slave with deferred emancipation) from another usurper: if the mudabbar dies or escapes while with the second, the first, if he pays compensation, can recover it from the second even though he never owned the mudabbar. Likewise, the witnesses to a kitabah (contract of manumission) if they rescind after judgment and pay its value: they recover the substitute of the kitabah from the mukatab (slave) and do not own his person; because we said the cause of ownership has occurred, and the original potentially owns, so when ownership is not established, the substitute takes its place. As for Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him), he said: The witnesses are cause of destruction judicially by way of indirect causation, and the heir is a direct destroyer in reality, and both are equal regarding liability for blood money. When the heir does not recover, the witnesses also do not recover. Unlike the witnesses of error (khata'), for if they pay and the alleged murdered comes alive, they recover because they are not liable for destruction, but for what they imposed on the heir; so when they pay, the heir becomes the destroyer against them, because what is guaranteed there is property, and property is potentially ownable. The response to their (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad's) argument: Ownership of the original destroyed—blood—is not legislated at all and cannot be owned, so the cause is not attached to it and the substitute is invalid. And because the substitute follows the original, and the original is blood that is destroyed, and ownership of blood is qisas (retaliation), and the original itself is not guaranteed if it becomes owned, so likewise its substitute. In the mudabbar case: the original is guaranteed if it is owned undoubtedly, so its substitute is likewise.
Source pages p. 679

Commentary
This passage establishes the foundational usul principle regarding substitution (khilafah). The author begins by noting that detailed derivations are found in the larger works (mabsut), but here only the core principle is indicated: substitution is only established by nass (explicit text) or dalalat al-nass (indication of text). The essential condition is that the original (asl) must be absent at present but potentially existent (muhtamal al-wujud). This possibility allows the cause (sabab) to be effectively attached to the original, making the substitute valid. If the original cannot possibly exist, no substitute is established. An example: a false oath (yamīn ghamūs) where the cause for expiation (kaffarah) does not arise because the original (i.e., the legitimate oath that could be expiated) is impossible. In contrast, matters like 'touching the sky' (mas al-sama')—a technical example of something impossible in reality but legally possible—do allow substitutes. The principle applies to numerous cases, some already mentioned concerning one who accepts Islam at the end of prayer time. Then the text presents a detailed intra-madhhab dispute. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad apply the principle to the case of witnesses who testify that Zayd killed Amr; then the alleged victim (Amr) appears alive, while the accused (Zayd) has already been executed. The heir (wali) chooses to make the witnesses liable for blood money (diyah). Abu Yusuf and Muhammad argue that the witnesses can recover from the heir: because the cause of ownership (sabab al-milk) existed (transgression and liability), and the subject (blood) is legally capable of being owned (though impossible in reality, it is possible in legal hypothetical, like 'touching the sky'). Hence the substitute (diyah) operates when the original (blood ownership/having the right to qisas) cannot be implemented. Similar analogies are drawn: the case of the usurper of a mudabbar (where the original—ownership of the slave—is possible, so the substitute is allowed), and the case of witnesses to a kitabah (manumission contract) who revoke after judgment. Abu Hanifa disagrees. He views the witnesses as causing loss indirectly (tasbib) and the heir as direct destroyer (mubasharah); both are equally liable. Since the heir cannot recover (because he chose to hold witnesses liable), the witnesses also cannot recover. He distinguishes the case of witnesses to accidental killing (khata') where the witnesses pay and can recover because they are not liable for destruction but for what they imposed—the liability is for property (diyah), which is potentially ownable. The text then gives the Hanafi response (favored by the author) against Abu Yusuf and Muhammad: Ownership of blood itself is not legislated in Islam (it is not a commodity), so the cause cannot attach to it, and the substitute is invalid. Moreover, the substitute mimics the original; the original (blood) is not guaranteed if owned (qisas is not monetary), so its substitute (diyah) cannot be treated as a property substitute here. For the mudabbar, the original is indeed guaranteed (since ownership of a slave is recognized), so the substitute is valid.

Key Terms
الغَموس (al-ghamus): A false oath sworn deliberately and knowingly; it is considered sinful and no expiation is accepted.
مس السماء (mas al-sama'): A technical example of something legally possible but physically impossible, used to illustrate that ibahat (permissibility) can be based on probability.
خلف (khalaf): Substitute; a legally recognized replacement for an original when the original is absent but potentially existent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi principle of khilafah, why is expiation not required for a false oath (yamīn ghamūs)?
A) Because false oaths are not mentioned in the Quran.
B) Because the original (legitimate oath) cannot possibly exist in this case.
C) Because the person did not mean to swear.
D) Because expiation is only for unintentional oaths.

Key Takeaway
Substitution in Hanafi usul requires the original to be legally possible; if the original is impossible in Shari'ah, no substitute operates.

The Five Categories of Condition in Hanafi Usul al-Fiqh
Classifying the five types of condition in Hanafi usul al-fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the five categories of condition (shart) as defined by al-Bazdawi and apply the Hanafi principle that a pure condition nullifies the cause, while a condition as a pure sign does not.

Core Idea
A pure condition prevents the cause; a sign does not: classification determines legal effect.

Conditions (shurut) are central to Hanafi legal theory. Al-Bazdawi classifies them into five types, each with distinct legal impact. This lesson parses these categories and applies them to Quranic examples, revealing how the Hanafi school differs from the Shafi'i on the effect of conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five categories
Read the opening division and list each type with its Arabic name: شرط محض, شرط له حكم العلل, شرط له حكم الأسباب, شرط اسماً لا حكماً, شرط بمعنى العلامة الخالصة.
2
Analyze the pure condition (shart mahd)
Understand its definition: it prevents the cause (علة) from existing until the condition is met. Note examples from divorce (conditional talaq) and acts of worship (knowledge as condition).
The Hanafi effect: non-existence of the cause (انعدام العلة).
3
Contrast the Hanafi vs Shafi'i effect
The Hanafi view: condition nullifies cause. Shafi'i view: condition defers the ruling (تراخي الحكم). Note the practical consequence: in Hanafi, obligation begins immediately when condition is met; in Shafi'i, it is deferred.
4
Examine the remaining four types
For each: condition with ruling of causes (acts like a cause), condition with ruling of reasons (acts like a sabab), condition in name only (metaphorical, no effect), condition as pure sign (indicates something without affecting the cause).
5
Apply to Quranic verses
Analyze the examples: Quran 24:33 (فَكَاتِبُوهُمْ إِنْ عَلِمْتُمْ فِيهِمْ خَيْرًا) and 4:101 (فَلَيْسَ عَلَيْكُمْ جُنَاحٌ أَن نَقْصُرُوا مِنَ الصَّلَوَة إِنْ خِفْتُمْ). Show that the conditional clause is not a pure condition but a sign indicating recommendation or specific circumstance.
Use istihsan to reinterpret the apparent condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو خمسة أقسام:
1 - شرط محض.
2 - وشرط له حكم العلل.
3 - وشرط له حكم الأسباب.
4 - وشرط اسماً، لا حكماً، فكان مجازاً في الباب.
5 - وشرط هو بمعنى العلامة الخالصة.
أما الشرط المحض فما يمتنع به وجود العلة، فإذا وُجد الشرط: وُجدت العلة، فيصير الوجود مضافاً إلى الشرط، دون الوجوب.
وذلك في كل تعليق بحرف من حروف الشرط، مثل: إن دخلت الدار فأنت طالق وكلما دخلت الدار، وما أشبه ذلك.
وذلك داخل في العبادات والمعاملات، ألا يُرى أن وجوب العبادات يتعلق بأسبابها، ثم يتوقف ذلك على شرط العلم، حتى إن النص النازل لا حكم له قبل العلم من المخاطَب فإن من أسلم في دار الحرب: لم يلزمه شيء من الشرائع قبل العلم، فصارت الأسباب والعلل بمنزلة المعدوم؛ لعدم الشرط.
وكذلك ركن العبادات ينعدم لعدم شروطها، وهي للصلاة النية، والطهارة.
وكذلك ركن النكاح، وهو الإيجاب والقبول ينعدم عند عدم شرطه وهو الإشهاد عليه، وقد ذكرنا أن أثر الشرط عندنا: انعدام العلة، وعند الشافعي رحمه الله: تراخي الحكم.
وكذلك هذا في كل الشروط، وإنما يُعرف الشرط بصيغته، أو بدلالته، وقط لا تنفك صيغته معناه عن.
فأما قول الله تعالى: ﴿فَكَاتِبُوهُمْ إِنْ عَلِمْتُمْ فِيهِمْ خَيْرًا﴾ [النور/33]: فقد قال بعضهم: هو شرط عادةً، وليس كذلك.
وهذا قول بأنه لغو، وكتابُ الله تعالى منزه عن مثله، ولكن أدنى درجات الأمر: استحباب المأمور به واستحباب الكتابة متعلق بهذا الشرط، لا يوجد إلا به، وينعدم قبله.
فأما الإباحة فتستغني عنه والمراد بالأمر الاستحباب، ألا يُرى أن قوله تعالى: ﴿وَآتُوهُم مِّن مَّالِ اللَّهِ الَّذِي آتَاكُمْ﴾ [النور/33]: سنة واستحباب.
وكذلك قوله تعالى: ﴿فَلَيْسَ عَلَيْكُمْ جُنَاحٌ أَن نَقْصُرُوا مِنَ الصَّلَوَة إِنْ خِفْتُمْ﴾ [النساء/101]: ليس بشرط عادةً، بل هو شرط أريد به حقيقة ما وضع له؛ لأن المراد بالنص قصر الأحوال، وهو أن يومئ على الدابة، أو يُخفّف القراءة والتسبيح. ألا يُرى إلى قوله تعالى: ﴿فَإِنْ خِفْتُمْ فَرِجَالًا أَوْ رُكْبَانًا فَإِذَا أَمِنتُمْ فَاذْكُرُوا اللَّهَ كَمَا عَلَمَكُم﴾ [البقرة/239]، وقال تعالى: ﴿فَإِذَا اطْمَأْنَنتُمْ
And it is five divisions: 1 - Pure condition. 2 - A condition that has the ruling of causes. 3 - A condition that has the ruling of reasons. 4 - A condition in name, not in ruling, so it is metaphorical in the chapter. 5 - A condition that means a pure sign. As for the pure condition, it is that by which the existence of the cause is prevented, so when the condition exists, the cause exists, so existence is attributed to the condition, not obligation. And that is in every suspension with a conditional letter, such as: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced' and 'Whenever you enter the house' and the like. And that is included in acts of worship and transactions. Do you not see that the obligation of acts of worship is attached to their causes, then that depends on the condition of knowledge, so that a revealed text has no ruling before knowledge by the addressee. For if someone accepts Islam in dar al-harb, nothing of the laws is incumbent upon him before knowledge, so causes and reasons become as if nonexistent due to the lack of the condition. Similarly, the pillar of acts of worship ceases to exist due to lack of its conditions, and for prayer they are intention and purity. Similarly, the pillar of marriage, which is offer and acceptance, ceases to exist when its condition is absent, which is witnessing it. And we have mentioned that the effect of the condition according to us is the non-existence of the cause, and according to al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, is the deferral of the ruling. And this applies to all conditions. The condition is known by its wording or by its indication, and its wording is never separated from its meaning. As for the saying of Allah Most High: 'Then write a contract with them if you know good in them' [an-Nur 24:33], some said it is a customary condition, but it is not. This is a saying that it is meaningless, and the Book of Allah is exalted above such. But the lowest level of the command is recommendation of what is commanded, and the recommendation of writing is attached to this condition; it does not exist except with it, and it is nonexistent before it. As for permissibility, it is independent of it. And the intended meaning of the command is recommendation. Do you not see that His saying: 'And give them from the wealth of Allah which He has given you' [an-Nur 24:33] is a sunnah and recommendation. Similarly, His saying: 'Then there is no sin upon you to shorten the prayer if you fear' [an-Nisa' 4:101] is not a customary condition, but rather a condition intended in its true sense, because the intended meaning of the text is the shortening of circumstances, which is to gesture on the mount or to lighten the recitation and glorification. Do you not see His saying: 'If you are in fear, then (pray) on foot or riding; then when you are safe, remember Allah as He has taught you' [al-Baqarah 2:239], and He said: 'Then when you are secure...'
Source pages p. 695

Commentary
This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul classifies the condition (shart) into five types. The first is the pure condition (shart mahd), which prevents the existence of the cause (illah) until the condition is met. When the condition exists, the cause comes into existence, but the ruling (hukm) is attributed to the condition, not to obligation. Examples include conditional divorce (ta'liq al-talaq) and similar statements. The second type is a condition that has the ruling of causes (shart lahu hukm al-'ilal), meaning it acts like a cause in some respects. The third is a condition that has the ruling of reasons (shart lahu hukm al-asbab), akin to a reason (sabab). The fourth is a condition only in name (shart isman), with no actual effect, making it metaphorical. The fifth is a condition that is a pure sign ('alamah khalisah), indicating something without affecting the cause. The author then elaborates on the pure condition, emphasizing that it is used in acts of worship and transactions. He gives examples from prayer and marriage, noting that the pillars (arkan) are nullified in the absence of conditions. He contrasts the Hanafi view that the condition nullifies the cause with the Shafi'i view that it merely defers the ruling. Finally, he analyzes Quranic verses to show that some conditional clauses are not real conditions but recommendations or signs, such as the verse on writing contracts (al-Nur 24:33) and the verse on shortening prayer (al-Nisa' 4:101). He argues that these are not customary conditions but rather conditions intended in their true linguistic sense, serving as indicators for recommended actions or specific circumstances.

Key Terms
شرط محض (shart mahd): Pure condition that prevents the existence of the cause
شرط له حكم العلل (shart lahu hukm al-‘ilal): Condition that has the ruling of causes
شرط له حكم الأسباب (shart lahu hukm al-asbab): Condition that has the ruling of reasons
شرط اسماً لا حكماً (shart isman la hukman): Condition in name only, metaphorical
شرط بمعنى العلامة الخالصة (shart bi-ma‘na al-‘alamah al-khalisah): Condition as a pure sign

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Bazdawi, what is the effect of a pure condition (shart mahd) according to the Hanafis?
A) It defers the ruling until the condition is fulfilled
B) It nullifies the existence of the cause
C) It makes the ruling conditional
D) It is merely a sign

Key Takeaway
Conditions are not uniform; the Hanafi school classifies them into five types, each with specific legal effects, and applies this classification to Quranic verses to avoid imposing obligation where only recommendation is intended.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate
8 min
Condition vs. Cause in Hanafi Usul
Usul rule distinguishing condition and cause with applied examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a condition (shart) and a cause ('illa) and apply the principle that a condition can serve as a cause when not opposed by a stronger cause.

Core Idea
A condition relates to existence, a cause to obligation; when no cause opposes, the condition steps in as cause.

In Hanafi usul, the relationship between conditions and causes is foundational for deriving rulings. This passage explains that while conditions (shart) pertain to the existence of a ruling, causes ('illa) pertain to its obligation. However, when a condition is not contradicted by a cause, it can itself function as a cause. This principle is applied to scenarios of witness testimony, liability, and even physical causation like digging a well.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish shart and 'illa
Understand that shart affects existence (wujud) while 'illa affects obligation (wujub). The condition is necessary for the existence of the ruling, but the cause is the reason the ruling is binding.
This is the core conceptual distinction.
2
Identify the principle
The text states: 'Every condition not opposed by a cause can be a cause.' This is the key rule. When no cause exists, the condition can act as the basis for the ruling.
The condition is subsumed if a cause exists.
3
Apply to witness liability
When witnesses of both condition and cause retract, the cause witnesses bear liability because they proved the effective cause. The condition witnesses are not liable because their testimony is overridden.
Example: oath witnesses vs. fetter-weight witnesses.
4
Analyze intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Hanifa holds that condition witnesses are liable if only they retract because judgment is effective externally and internally. The Sahibayn hold that judgment is only external, so the condition witnesses did not cause the emancipation.
The disagreement hinges on whether the judgment affects internal reality.
5
Extend to physical causality
The principle is applied to digging a well: weight is the cause of falling, walking is the means, but the earth prevents; digging removes the preventer, so it is a condition that becomes the effective cause for the fall.
Illustrates how conditions can function as causes in natural causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَأَقِيمُوا الصَّلوةَ النساء / ۱۰۳ ، وقَصْرُ الأحوال يتعلق بقيام الخوف عياناً، لا بنفس السفر.
فأما قوله تعالى: ﴿وَرَبِّبُكُمُ الَّتِي فِي حُجُورِكُم مِّن نِّسَا بِكُمْ النساء / ٢٣ : فلم يذكر الحجور شرطاً، وإنما الشرط: قوله تعالى: ﴿فَإِن لَّمْ تَكُونُوا دَخَلْتُم بِهِنَّ فَلَا جُنَاحَ عَلَيْكُمْ ﴾ النساء /٢٣ ، وهو شرط اسماً وحكماً.
وكذلك دلالة الشرط لا تنفك عن مدلوله وذلك مثل قول الرجل : المرأة التي أتزوجها طالق ثلاثاً هذا الكلام بمعنى الشرط دلالةً؛ لوقوع الوصف في النكرة، ولو وقع في العين لما صلح دلالةً، ونص الشرط يجمع الوجهين.
٢- وأما الشرط الذي هو في حكم العلل : فإن كل شرط لم تعارضه علة : صلح أن يكون علة يُضاف إليه الحكم، ومتى عارضته علة : لم يصلح علة، وذلك لما قلنا إن الشرط يتعلق به الوجود دون الوجوب، فصار شبيهاً بالعلل والعلل أصول لكنها لما لم تكن عللاً بذواتها : استقام أن تخلفها الشروط. وهذا أصل كبير لعلمائنا رحمهم الله، فقد قالوا في شهود الشرط واليمين إذا رجعوا جميعا بعد الحكم إن الضمان يجب على شهود اليمين ؛ لأنهم شهود العلة.
وكذلك العلة والسبب إذا اجتمعا سقط حكم السبب، كشهود التخيير والاختيار إذا اجتمعوا في الطلاق والعتاق ثم رجعوا بعد الحكم: فإن الضمان على شهود الاختيار ؛ لأنه هو العلة، والتخيير سبب. فأما إذا سلم الشرط عن معارضة العلة صلح علةً؛ لما قلنا، وذلك مثل قول علمائنا رحمهم الله في رجل قيَّد عبده، ثم حلف، فقال: إن كان قيده عشرة أرطال فهو حر، ثم قال : وإن حَلَّه أحدٌ من الناس فهو حر، فشهد شاهدان أن القيد عشرة أرطال فقضى القاضي بعتقه، ثم حَلَّه فوَزَنَه فإذا هو ثمانية أرطال : إن الشاهدين يضمنان قيمته في قول أبي حنيفة رحمه الله ؛ لأن القضاء بالإعتاق ينفذ عنده ظاهراً وباطناً، فقد وجب العتق بشهادتهما.
وعندهما لا يضمنان؛ لأن القضاء لم ينفذ في الباطن، فوقع العتق بحل القيد، وهذان الشاهدان أثبتا شرط العتق لا علة العتق ومع ضمنا من قبل أن علة العتق لا تصلح لضمان العتق، وهو يمينُ المولى، فجعل الشرط علةً .
وفي مسألة رجوع الفريقين إيجاب كلمة العتق يصلح لضمان العدوان؛ لأنها تثبت بطريق التعدي، فلم يجعل الشرط علةً، وإذا رجع شهود الشرط وحدَهم : يجب أن يضمنوا؛ لما قلنا.
فأما شهود الإحصان إذا رجعوا فلا يضمنون بحال عندنا، خلافاً لزفر رحمه الله ؛ لأن الإحصان لا يتعلق به وجوب ولا وجود، على ما نبيِّن إن شاء الله تعالى.
-
وعلى هذا الأصل: حَفْرُ البئر هو شرط في الحقيقة؛ لأن الثقل علة السقوط، والمشي سبب محض، لكن الأرض كانت ممسكةً مانعة عمل الثقل، فيكون حفر البئر إزالة للمانع.
So establish prayer (Quran 4:103). And the restriction of conditions relates to the existence of fear tangibly, not to travel itself. As for His statement: 'and your stepdaughters who are in your guardianship from your women' (Quran 4:23), He did not mention guardianship as a condition. Rather the condition is His statement: 'If you have not entered into them, then there is no blame upon you' (Quran 4:23), and it is a condition in name and ruling. Likewise, the indication of the condition cannot be separated from its denotation. For example, a man's saying: 'The woman I marry is thrice divorced.' This speech has the meaning of a condition by indication, because the description occurs in an indefinite noun; if it had occurred in a definite noun it would not be sound as an indication, and the explicit text of the condition combines both aspects. 2- As for the condition that is in the position of causes: For every condition that is not opposed by a cause, it is valid to be a cause to which the ruling is attributed. But when a cause opposes it, it is not valid as a cause. This is because we have said that the condition relates to existence (wujud) without obligation (wujub), so it becomes similar to causes. And causes are fundamentals, but since they are not causes by themselves, it is plausible that conditions override them. This is a great principle for our scholars (may Allah have mercy on them). They have said regarding witnesses of the condition and the oath when all of them retract after judgment: The liability (daman) falls on the witnesses of the oath because they are the witnesses of the cause. Likewise, the cause and the means (sabab) when they combine, the ruling of the means drops. For example, witnesses of choice (takhyir) and free choice (ikhtiyar) when they combine in divorce and manumission, then retract after judgment: The liability is on the witnesses of free choice because it is the cause, and choice is a means. As for when the condition is free from opposition by a cause, it is valid as a cause, as we have said. This is like the statement of our scholars (may Allah have mercy on them) about a man who fettered his slave then made an oath saying: 'If his fetters weigh ten ratls, he is free,' then said: 'And if any person releases him, he is free.' Two witnesses testified that the fetter weighed ten ratls, and the judge ruled his freedom. Then [another] released him and weighed it, and it turned out to be eight ratls. The two witnesses are liable for his value according to Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him), because the judgment of freedom is effective in his view both externally and internally, so the emancipation became established by their testimony. According to the two companions [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad], they are not liable because the judgment was not effective internally, so the emancipation occurred by the release of the fetter. These two witnesses established the condition of emancipation, not the cause of emancipation. And if they were [considered] liable from before, it is because the cause of emancipation is not valid for liability of emancipation, and it is the master's oath. So the condition was made a cause. In the case of the retraction of both groups [of witnesses], the effect of the word of emancipation is valid for liability of transgression because it is established by the way of aggression, so the condition was not made a cause. And if the witnesses of the condition alone retract, they must be liable, as we have said. As for witnesses of ihsan [having previously married] if they retract, they are not liable in any case in our school, contrary to Zufar (may Allah have mercy on him), because ihsan does not relate to either obligation or existence, as we will explain if Allah wills. Based on this principle: digging a well is in reality a condition, because weight is the cause of falling, and walking is a pure means, but the earth was holding and preventing the operation of weight, so digging the well is removal of the impediment.
Source pages p. 697

Commentary
This passage is a rule from Hanafi usul al-fiqh distinguishing between a condition (shart) and a cause ('illa). The author begins by citing Quranic examples to illustrate conditions in legal texts. The key distinction is that a condition relates to existence (wujud) — the conditioned thing exists when the condition is met — whereas a cause relates to obligation (wujub) — the cause makes the ruling binding. When a condition and a cause conflict, the cause prevails because it is stronger; the condition's effect on existence is secondary. The text then applies this principle to cases of witnesses retracting after judgment. If witnesses of both a condition and a cause (e.g., oath) retract, liability falls on the witnesses of the cause because they directly prove the cause of the ruling. Similarly, if a cause and a means (sabab) combine, the ruling of the means drops. The example of the fettered slave shows a disagreement: Abu Hanifa holds that the witnesses' testimony caused the emancipation (effective both externally and internally), so they are liable if they lied; his two companions, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, hold that the judgment was only external, so the emancipation was actually due to the release of the fetter (the condition), and the witnesses only proved the condition, not the cause, so they are not liable for the value. However, if only the condition witnesses retract, they are liable according to all because they caused the emancipation through transgression. Witnesses of ihsan (chastity status) are never liable because ihsan is neither a condition nor a cause for the ruling on stoning. Finally, the principle is extended to digging a well: weight is the cause of falling, walking is the means, but the earth prevents; digging removes the preventer, making it a condition for the fall. Thus, the condition (digging) is akin to a cause when not opposed by a real cause.

Key Terms
شَرْط (shart): A condition is something whose existence is necessary for the existence of a ruling, but it does not obligate the ruling itself; it relates to wujud (existence).
عِلَّة (illa): A cause is that which necessitates the ruling in terms of obligation (wujub); it is a defining attribute that the lawgiver has made the basis of a ruling.
سَبَب (sabab): A means (sabab) is a conduit for the ruling, but weaker than a cause; when both a cause and a means combine, the ruling of the means is dropped.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case where witnesses of both a condition and a cause (such as an oath) retract after judgment, who must pay damages according to the Hanafi principle?
A) The witnesses of the condition alone
B) The witnesses of the cause alone
C) Both groups equally
D) Neither group

Key Takeaway
A condition (shart) can function as a cause ('illa) for a ruling only when no genuine cause opposes it; otherwise, the cause prevails.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
